Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Purification by Complete Transformation (Istihalah)
Ruling with proof via qiyas and analogies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purification by istihalah and apply the qiyas to determine purity of transformed najis substances.

Core Idea
Impurity follows the original substance; complete transformation removes impurity.

This lesson addresses a key principle in Hanafi taharah: that najasah can become pure if its substance undergoes a complete transformation (istihalah). The author presents examples like meat turning into salt or burning to ash, and supports the ruling with rational proof and analogies from fiqh, such as the transformation of nutfah (impure) to mudghah (pure) and wine into vinegar. Understanding this principle is crucial for correctly applying rulings of purity in cases of chemical change.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the matn: najasah becomes pure if its substance transforms completely. Note the examples: salt, earth, ash.
The transformation must be complete, not merely a physical change like drying.
2
Analyze the rationale
The commentator argues that impurity is attached to the specific substance (e.g., meat, bone). When that substance ceases to exist, the impurity ruling ceases. The whole substance disappearing is more conclusive than partial disappearance.
This is a rational (aqli) proof that the ruling follows the reality.
3
Examine the qiyas examples
The text gives two analogies: (1) nutfah (impure) to mudghah (pure) in embryology, and (2) pure juice to impure wine to pure vinegar. These show that transformation changes the ruling.
These examples are from established fiqh rulings, serving as the asl for the qiyas.
4
Extract the illah (effective cause)
The common factor in all cases is istihalah: complete transformation of the substance itself. This is the operative cause for purification.
The illah is jaliyyah (clear) and extracted by tahqiq al-manat.
5
Apply to new cases
The student should be able to take any scenario where a najis substance undergoes a complete chemical change (e.g., dead animal becomes salt) and rule that the new substance is pure.
Beware of incomplete transformations like mere decomposition or dissolving.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتَطْهُرُ نجاسة استحالت عينها، كأن صارت مِلْحاً، أو احترقت بالنَّار. قال الشارح: وتَظْهرُ نجاسة استحالَتْ عينُهَا، كأن صارت ملحاً أو تراباً أو أطروناً، أو احترقت بالنَّارِ، فيصير رمادها طاهراً على الصحيح، لأن الشرع رتب وصف النجاسة على تلك الحقيقة، وتنتفي الحقيقة بانتفاء بعض أجزاء مفهومها، فكيف بالكل. فإن الملح غير اللحم والعظم، فإذا صارت ملحاً، ترتب عليه حكمه، ونظيره في النطفة نجسة، ثم تصير علقة وهي نجسة، وتصير مضغة فتطهر، والعصير طاهر فيصير خمراً فينجس، ثم يصير خلاً، فيطهر، فعرفنا استحالة العين يستتبع زوال الوصف المرتب عليها.
And najasah whose substance has undergone transformation becomes pure, such as when it becomes salt or is burned by fire. The commentator said: And it appears that najasah whose substance has transformed—such as when it becomes salt, earth, or natron, or is burned by fire—and its ash becomes pure, according to the correct view. Because the Sacred Law attached the description of najasah to that actual substance, and the substance ceases to exist with the disappearance of some parts of its concept, so how much more with the entire substance? For salt is different from meat and bone; so when it becomes salt, its ruling applies. An analogy is the nutfah which is impure, then becomes a clot which is impure, then becomes a mudghah and becomes pure; and juice is pure, then becomes wine and becomes impure, then becomes vinegar and becomes pure. Thus we know that transformation of the substance entails removal of the description attached to it.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage discusses the principle of istihalah (transformation) as a means of purification. The matn states that najasah becomes pure if its substance changes entirely, such as turning into salt or being burned to ash. The commentator elaborates that the ruling of impurity is attached to the specific substance (e.g., meat, blood). Once that substance is no longer present (e.g., turned into salt), the impurity ruling ceases. The proof includes rational reasoning (the whole is greater than its parts) and analogies from fiqh: the development of the embryo (impure nutfah becomes pure mudghah) and the transformation of wine into vinegar. This establishes that istihalah is a valid cause for purification in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
استحالة (istihalah): Complete transformation of the substance of a thing from its original nature to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does najasah become pure through istihalah?
A) When it is dried by the sun.
B) When its substance completely transforms, such as turning to salt or ash.
C) When it is diluted with water.
D) When it is removed from its original place.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that istihalah (complete transformation) purifies najasah because impurity is tied to the original substance, not the new one.

Ruling on Condensed Vapor from Najasah
Ruling on condensed vapor from impure places with istihsan-based exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on condensed vapor from places with najasah and explain the role of istihsan in overriding qiyas.

Core Idea
Vapor from najasah is pure by necessity (istihsan) but becomes impure if intentionally distilled.

This lesson explores a practical case where the general qiyas rule would declare vapor from impure places as impure, but the Hanafi school applies istihsan to grant an exception due to necessity. Understanding this ruling illustrates the flexibility of Hanafi fiqh in accommodating everyday hardships.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling by qiyas
The act is the condensation of vapor from a latrine or stable containing animal dung, which then drips. By qiyas, since the vapor originates from najasah, it should be impure and render surfaces it touches impure.
2
Recognize the istihsan exception
The Hanafi school overrides the qiyas ruling by istihsan, declaring the drip pure. The basis is necessity (darurah) and the difficulty (or impossibility) of avoiding such vapor in daily life.
This is a classic example of istihsan overriding qiyas.
3
Identify the condition for the exception
The exception applies only when the vapor is unavoidable. If someone intentionally extracts or distills the najasah (e.g., to produce a spirit), then the resulting liquid is impure because the excuse of necessity no longer exists.
The text likens this to the distillation of wine dregs (called 'araqi) which is impure and haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه: إذا ارتفع بخار الكنيف والإصطبل الذي فيه روث الدواب، فاستجمد في الكوة أو الجدار، ثم ذاب، وقطر، نَجَّس ما أصابه، لاجتماع الجمد من أجزاء النجاسة قياساً، ولا ينجس استحساناً، وكذا الحمام، إذا أُهريق فيه النجاسة فعرّق حيطانه وكواته فتقاطر، وجه الاستحسان الضرورة وعـــدم التحرز أو تعسره، فعلى هذا لو استقطر النجاسة، فالقاطر نجس، لانتفاء الضرورة، كالمستقطر من دردي الخمر - ويسمى بالعرقي في بلاد الروم- نجس حرام، كسائر المحرمات. فرع: بيض ما لا يؤكل، قيل: نجس كلحمه وقيل: طاهر.
Warning: If the vapor of a latrine or stable which contains animal dung rises and condenses in a window or wall, then melts and drips, it renders what it hits impure by analogy, but it does not render impure by istihsan. Similarly, a bathhouse: if najasah is poured into it and its walls and windows sweat and drip, the basis of istihsan is necessity and the difficulty or impossibility of avoidance. Based on this, if one intentionally extracts the najasah (by distillation), then the dripping is impure, because necessity is absent, like the extract of wine dregs - called 'araqi in the lands of Rum - which is impure and unlawful, like other prohibited things. Branch: Eggs of what is not eaten: some say it is impure like its meat, some say it is pure.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage covers two separate issues. First, it discusses the ruling on vapor from places containing najasah (like latrines or stables) that condenses and drips. By qiyas (analogy), it should be impure because it originates from najasah. However, istihsan (juridical preference) dictates that it is not impure due to necessity (the difficulty of avoiding such vapor in daily life). If someone intentionally distills the najasah (e.g., to make a spirit), then the product is impure because the excuse of necessity no longer applies. The commentator gives the example of 'araqi (a spirit made from wine dregs) which is impure and haram. Second, a branch issue: eggs of non-edible birds (like birds of prey) are disputed—some say they are impure like their meat, others say they are pure.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference that deviates from qiyas due to necessity or equity.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical deduction used to extend a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the drip from a bathhouse where najasah has been poured, if the dripping occurs naturally and unavoidably?
A) It is impure and renders surfaces impure.
B) It is pure because of necessity (istihsan).
C) It is pure only if the najasah was diluted.
D) It is impure if the najasah was animal dung.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to rule that unavoidable condensed vapor from najasah is pure, but intentional extraction makes it impure.

Ruling on Eggs of Non-Edible Birds
Branch ruling on eggs of non-edible birds with two opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two opinions within the Hanafi school regarding the eggs of non-edible birds and identify the basis of each.

Core Idea
Eggs of non-edible birds: some say impure like meat, some say pure; no single preferred view is given.

This lesson addresses a branch issue from the previous one: the ruling on eggs of birds whose meat is not eaten (e.g., birds of prey). The text presents two opinions without explicit tarjih, illustrating that not all issues have a single authoritative position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
The subject is eggs of birds whose meat is not considered halal to eat (e.g., eagles, hawks, crows).
2
Learn the first opinion
Some scholars say these eggs are impure (najis) like the meat of such birds, meaning they carry the same ruling as the flesh.
This is based on the principle that the egg derives from the bird's body.
3
Learn the second opinion
Other scholars say the eggs are pure (tahir), possibly because eggs are separate and not directly in contact with blood or flesh.
This opinion emphasizes the separate nature of eggs from the meat.
4
Note the lack of tarjih
The text does not indicate which opinion is stronger or mufta bihi; it simply reports both. This is a case of legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
In practice, a mufti may choose either view based on evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه: إذا ارتفع بخار الكنيف والإصطبل الذي فيه روث الدواب، فاستجمد في الكوة أو الجدار، ثم ذاب، وقطر، نَجَّس ما أصابه، لاجتماع الجمد من أجزاء النجاسة قياساً، ولا ينجس استحساناً، وكذا الحمام، إذا أُهريق فيه النجاسة فعرّق حيطانه وكواته فتقاطر، وجه الاستحسان الضرورة وعـــدم التحرز أو تعسره، فعلى هذا لو استقطر النجاسة، فالقاطر نجس، لانتفاء الضرورة، كالمستقطر من دردي الخمر - ويسمى بالعرقي في بلاد الروم- نجس حرام، كسائر المحرمات. فرع: بيض ما لا يؤكل، قيل: نجس كلحمه وقيل: طاهر.
Warning: If the vapor of a latrine or stable which contains animal dung rises and condenses in a window or wall, then melts and drips, it renders what it hits impure by analogy, but it does not render impure by istihsan. Similarly, a bathhouse: if najasah is poured into it and its walls and windows sweat and drip, the basis of istihsan is necessity and the difficulty or impossibility of avoidance. Based on this, if one intentionally extracts the najasah (by distillation), then the dripping is impure, because necessity is absent, like the extract of wine dregs - called 'araqi in the lands of Rum - which is impure and unlawful, like other prohibited things. Branch: Eggs of what is not eaten: some say it is impure like its meat, some say it is pure.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage covers two separate issues. First, it discusses the ruling on vapor from places containing najasah (like latrines or stables) that condenses and drips. By qiyas (analogy), it should be impure because it originates from najasah. However, istihsan (juridical preference) dictates that it is not impure due to necessity (the difficulty of avoiding such vapor in daily life). If someone intentionally distills the najasah (e.g., to make a spirit), then the product is impure because the excuse of necessity no longer applies. The commentator gives the example of 'araqi (a spirit made from wine dregs) which is impure and haram. Second, a branch issue: eggs of non-edible birds (like birds of prey) are disputed—some say they are impure like their meat, others say they are pure.

Key Terms
بيض ما لا يؤكل (bayd ma la yu'kal): Eggs of birds whose meat is not eaten (e.g., birds of prey).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the text say about eggs of non-edible birds?
A) They are all impure.
B) They are all pure.
C) Some say impure, some say pure.
D) They are impure only if fertilized.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on eggs of non-edible birds is disputed within the Hanafi school, with two valid opinions transmitted.

Purification of Mani: Dry by Scraping, Wet by Washing
Ruling on purification of mani with proofs and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for purifying dry and wet mani and explain the primary evidence.

Core Idea
Dry mani is purified by scraping; wet mani by washing — the proof is the Prophet's command.

Mani is considered impure (najis) in the Hanafi school, but its purification method is lenient due to hardship. This lesson examines the ruling: dry mani is purified by scraping (fark), wet mani by washing. We will look at the textual proofs and note an intra-madhhab disagreement regarding the body.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The method of purifying mani (dry vs wet). The hukm is that scraping suffices for dry, washing for wet.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the primary evidence: the hadith 'Wash it wet, scrape it dry' and the supporting ahadith from Aisha and Ammar.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The explicit command indicates obligation of washing for wet and permissibility/adequacy of scraping for dry.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Within the Hanafi school, Abu Hanifa held that dry mani on the body is not purified by scraping due to moisture; the zahir al-riwayah holds it is. Also, al-Hasan considered mani like urine (requiring washing).
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the zahir al-riwayah: scraping purifies both garment and body, due to necessity and ease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويطهر المني الجافُ بفَرْكِهِ عن الثَّوب، ولو كان جديداً مبطناً، (وَ) عن (البَدَن) بفركه في ظاهر الرواية، وعن أبي حنيفة: لا يطهر البدن بالفرك لرطوبته، وظاهر الرواية طهارته، للضرورة، إذا خرج المني بعد الاستنجاء بالماء، أما لو بال، ولم يستنج، فلا يطهر المني إلا بالغسل، لعدمها، وقيل: لو بال ولم ينتشر بوله على رأس الذكر، أو انتشر، ولكن خرج المني دفقاً من غير انتشار على محل البول، لا يحكم بتنجيسه به، لأنه لم يوجد سوى مروره على أثر البول في مجراه، ولا حكم له في الباطن. (وَيَطهُرُ) المني ( الرَّطب بغسله، لما روى مسلم عن عائشة : « أنَّه، كانَ يغسل المني، ثمَّ يخرجُ إلى الصَّلاة في ذلك الثوب، وأنا أنظر إلى أثر الغسل فيه » وحديث عمار أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام، قال: « إِنَّمَا يُغسلُ الثوب من خمس من الغائط، والبول، والقيء، والدم، والمني». وعن الحسن المني بمنزلة البول، ولأنه دم استحال بالنضج من حرارة الشهوة، ولهذا مَنْ كثر جماعه حتى فترت شهوته يخرج دماً أحمر، وإنما يطهر بالفرك لقوله : «اغسليه رطباً، وافركيه يابساً». وما ورد فيه من الإماطة محمول على أنه كان قليلاً، أو ليتمكن من الغسل.
And dry mani is purified by scraping it from the garment, even if it is new and lined, and from the body by scraping in the apparent narration. From Abu Hanifa: the body is not purified by scraping due to its moisture; the apparent narration is its purity, due to necessity, if the mani comes out after istinja with water. But if he urinated and did not perform istinja, then mani is not purified except by washing, because necessity is absent. And it is said: if he urinated and his urine did not spread to the head of the penis, or it did spread but the mani came out in a gush without spreading over the place of urine, it is not judged to be contaminated by it, because it only passed over the trace of urine in its passage, and there is no ruling for that in the interior. And wet mani is purified by washing, due to what Muslim narrated from Aisha: 'He used to wash mani, then go out to prayer in that garment, and I used to see the trace of washing in it.' And the hadith of Ammar that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The garment is only washed from five: feces, urine, vomit, blood, and mani.' And from al-Hasan: mani is like urine. And because it is blood that transformed by maturation from the heat of desire; for this reason, one who has much intercourse until his desire wanes produces red blood. It is purified by scraping due to his saying: 'Wash it when wet, and scrape it when dry.' And what has been mentioned about removal (imatah) is interpreted as being a small amount, or to enable washing.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage covers the purification of mani in detail. The matn states that dry mani is purified by scraping (from garment and body), and wet mani by washing. The commentator provides proofs and nuances. For dry mani on the body, there is an ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa says scraping does not purify the body due to moisture, but the zahir al-riwayah holds it does, based on necessity. The text also discusses cases where mani comes into contact with urine: if one urinates without istinja, mani is not purified except by washing; if the urine does not spread, or mani exits without touching the urine area, it remains pure. Proofs include the hadith of Aisha (washing mani) and the hadith of Ammar listing mani among five impure things. The analogy with blood transformed by desire is also mentioned. Finally, the hadith 'wash it wet, scrape it dry' is the primary evidence for scraping dry mani.

Key Terms
فرك (fark): Scraping or rubbing off dry mani from a surface until it is no longer visible.
المَني (al-mani): Semen, considered impure (najis) in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary proof for purifying dry mani by scraping?
A) The hadith of Ammar listing five impurities.
B) The hadith of Aisha showing the Prophet washing mani.
C) The hadith 'Wash it wet, scrape it dry'.
D) The analogy that mani is transformed blood.

Key Takeaway
Dry mani is purified by scraping; wet mani by washing — based on the Prophetic command and supported by Aisha's and Ammar's hadith.

Purification of Semen from Clothing
Ruling with proof on semen impurity and purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the impurity of semen, identify the proofs, and apply the method of purification (washing for wet, rubbing for dry) to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Semen is najis; wash when wet, rub when dry.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on semen as an impurity and the unique concession that dried semen can be purified by friction alone. This distinction reflects the school's method of balancing textual evidence (hadith) with practical ease, and it differs from other schools, particularly the Shafi'i view that semen is pure. Understanding this ruling is essential for daily taharah practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The purification of semen from clothing. State the Hanafi position: semen is najis (impure), and the garment must be purified before prayer.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The passage cites two main proofs: (1) the hadith of 'Ammar listing five impurities including semen, and (2) the action of the Prophet (ﷺ) washing semen from his garment. Identify the type of proof (Sunnah ahad).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How do these proofs establish impurity? The explicit mention of semen in the hadith as requiring washing indicates it is najis. The rational argument (qiyas) likens semen to transformed blood, supporting its impurity. Also, the instruction 'Wash it when wet, rub it when dry' shows the method.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds semen is pure (tahir). This lesson mentions the difference but focuses on the Hanafi position.
5
Evaluate the tarjih in practice
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that semen is impure and rubbing suffices for dry semen. This is confirmed by al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah and is the relied-upon view for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يغسل المني، ثمَّ يخرجُ إلى الصَّلاة في ذلك الثوب، وأنا أنظر إلى أثر الغسل فيه وحديث عمار أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام، قال: « إِنَّمَا يُغسلُ الثوب من خمس من الغائط، والبول، والقيء، والدم، والمني». وعن الحسن المني بمنزلة البول، ولأنه دم استحال بالنضج من حرارة الشهوة، ولهذا مَنْ كثر جَمَاعَهُ حتى فترت شهوته يخرج دماً أحمر، وإنما يطهر بالفرك لقوله : واغسليه رطباً، وافركيهِ يَابِسَاً. وما ورد فيه من الإماطة محمول على أنه كان قليلاً، أو ليتمكن من الغسل.
He washed the semen, then went out to prayer in that garment, and I was looking at the trace of washing in it. And the hadith of 'Ammar that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Indeed the garment is washed from five things: feces, urine, vomit, blood, and semen.' And from al-Hasan: semen is like urine, and because it is blood that changed due to ripeness from the heat of desire, and therefore one who has much intercourse until his desire weakens will emit red blood, and it is purified by rubbing because of his saying: 'Wash it when wet, and rub it when dry.' And what has been reported about removing it is interpreted as it was little, or to enable washing.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the purification of semen from clothing. The author begins by citing the practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) as narrated by Aisha: he would wash semen from his garment and then pray in it. Then he cites the hadith of 'Ammar enumerating five impure substances that necessitate washing: feces, urine, vomit, blood, and semen. This establishes semen as najis (impure). The passage then discusses the nature of semen: al-Hasan al-Basri equates it to urine, and a rational argument is given that semen is blood transformed by the heat of desire; if a person's desire weakens due to frequent intercourse, he may emit red blood. This analogy (qiyas) supports the impurity of semen. However, the method of purification differs: when semen is wet, it must be washed; when dry, it can be rubbed off, based on the Prophet's instruction 'Wash it when wet, and rub it when dry.' This is a concession specific to semen due to the difficulty of washing when dried. The final point clarifies that narrations mentioning mere removal (إماطة) refer to either a small amount or to make washing easier, not to negate the need for washing altogether.

Key Terms
المني (al-maniy): Semen; a bodily fluid that is considered impure (najis) in the Hanafi school, requiring purification of the garment before prayer.
الفرك (al-fark): Rubbing; the method of purifying dried semen from fabric by friction, valid according to the Hanafi school.
الغسل (al-ghusl): Washing; the required method for purifying wet semen from clothing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how is dried semen purified from a garment?
A) It must be washed with water.
B) It is purified by rubbing (al-fark).
C) It is not impure at all.
D) It requires repeating the prayer.

Key Takeaway
Semen is najis in the Hanafi school, but dried semen is purified by rubbing, based on a specific prophetic concession, easing daily practice.

Taharah — Purification

Purification of Hides by Tanning: Ruling and Exceptions
Ruling that tanning purifies hides of dead animals except pig and human

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tanning hides, identify the two exceptions (pig and human), and explain the evidence from hadith.

Core Idea
Tanning purifies any dead animal hide except pig and human — the hide becomes like any other pure skin.

This passage addresses a common practical issue: the hide of a dead animal. While its meat is impure, its skin can be transformed through tanning. The Prophet's Sunnah provides explicit proof that tanning removes impurity. However, two exceptional cases remain: the pig (due to intrinsic impurity) and humans (due to honor). Understanding this ruling allows us to use tanned leather from animals that died naturally or were not slaughtered Islamically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is tanning (dibagh) the hide of a dead animal. The ruling is that it becomes pure (tahir), permitting prayer, ablution, and use.
Tanning can be actual (using agents) or figurative (dusting/sun).
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'أيما إهاب دبغ فقد طهر' (Any hide that is tanned has become pure), narrated in Sahih Muslim. Additional narratives support this (Maymuna's goat, the waterskin).
The hadith is mutawatir in meaning, giving certainty in transmission (qati' al-thubut).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'طهر' indicates attainment of purity. The generality of 'أيما إهاب' includes all hides except those excluded by other evidence (pig and human).
The Hanafi principle: a general command (amm) is binding until specifically excluded.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is dispute over hides of dogs and elephants. Some (Zufar) said dog hide is not purified, but the soundest opinion (al-ashahh) is that it is purified because the dog is not intrinsically impure. Similarly for elephant.
Shafi'is hold that dog hide is not purified by tanning; the Hanafi view relies on the generality of the hadith.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text explicitly says 'على الأصح' (according to the soundest opinion) for dog and elephant, indicating that the mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that they become pure.
The authority weight is from the zahir al-riwayah of the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في طهارة جلود الميتة ونحوها يظهر جلد الميتة بالدباغة الحقيقية، كالقرظ، وبالحكمية، كالتتريب والتشميس، إلا جلد الخنزير والآدمي. ويطهر جلد الكلب الميت، لأنه ليس نجس العين، على الأصح. ولو كانت فيلاً، لأنه كسائر السباع في الأصح. والدباغة الحقيقية: كالقرظ، وهو ورق السلم أو ثمر السنط والعفص وقشور الرمان والشب. والحكمية: كالتتريب والتشميس والإلقاء في الهواء، فتجوز الصلاة فيه وعليه والوضوء منه، لقوله: 'أيما إهاب دبغ فقد طهر'، وفي صحيح مسلم: 'إذا دبغ الإهاب فقد طهر'. ولقوله في شاة ميمونة: 'هلا أخذتم إهابها فدبغتموه فانتفعتم به'. وأراد النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يتوضأ من سقاء، فقيل له: إنه ميتة فقال: 'دباغه مزيل حبته، أو نجسه أو رجسه'. وقال: 'استمتعوا بجلود الميتة إذا هي دبغت، تراباً كان أو رماداً أو ملحاً أو ما كان بعد أن يزيد صلاحه'. إلا جلد الخنزير لنجاسة عينه، والدباغة لإخراج الرطوبات في الجلد الطاهر بالأصالة، وهذا نجس العين، والآدمي لحرمته وكرامته، وإن حكم بطهارته بالدباغ، لا يجوز استعماله ولا الانتفاع به، كسائر أجزاء الآدمي.
Chapter on purification of hides of dead animals and similar: The hide of a dead animal becomes pure by actual tanning, like with qaraz (leaves of the acacia), and by figurative tanning, like covering with dust and sun exposure, except the hide of pig and human. And the hide of a dead dog is purified because it is not intrinsically impure, according to the soundest opinion. Even if it is an elephant, because it is like other wild beasts according to the soundest opinion. Actual tanning: like qaraz, which is the leaves of the samm tree or the fruit of the sant tree, gallnuts, pomegranate peels, and alum. Figurative tanning: like covering with dust, sun exposure, and leaving in the air. Thus prayer in it and on it and ablution from it are permissible, due to his saying: 'Any hide that is tanned has become pure.' And in Sahih Muslim: 'When a hide is tanned, it has become pure.' And his saying regarding the goat of Maymuna when they passed by it dead: 'Why did you not take its hide and tan it and benefit from it?' And the Prophet wanted to perform ablution from a waterskin, and it was said to him: 'It is dead.' He said: 'Its tanning removes its uncleanness,' or 'its impurity,' or 'its filth.' And he said: 'Benefit from the hides of dead animals when they are tanned, whether with soil, ash, salt, or anything that increases its goodness.' Except the hide of pig due to its intrinsic impurity – tanning extracts moisture from a skin that is originally pure, but this is intrinsically impure – and the human due to its sanctity and honor. Even if it is ruled pure by tanning, it is not permissible to use or benefit from it, like other parts of the human body.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the purification of hides from dead animals through tanning (dibagh). The author begins with the general ruling: any hide from a dead animal (except pig and human) becomes pure through tanning. 'Actual tanning' (الدباغة الحقيقية) uses tanning agents like qaraz (acacia leaves), gallnuts, pomegranate peels, and alum. 'Figurative tanning' (الدباغة الحكمية) includes covering with dust, sun exposure, or air-drying. The hide of a dog is purified because the Hanafi school holds that a dog is not intrinsically impure (najis al-ayn). The same applies to an elephant, as it is like other wild beasts. Multiple hadith are cited as proof: 'Any hide that is tanned has become pure' (Sahih Muslim), the story of Maymuna's goat, and the Prophet's approval of ablution from a tanned waterskin. These establish that tanning removes impurity. However, pig hide is excluded because the pig is intrinsically impure (najis al-ayn) by the Quranic verse 'Rijs' (impurity). Human hide is excluded due to the sanctity and honor of humans; even if tanned, it cannot be used or benefited from. The overall principle is that tanning transforms a impure hide into pure, but the hide must be from an animal that is not intrinsically impure.

Key Terms
الدباغة (al-dibaghah): Tanning; the process of treating animal hide with agents like qaraz (acacia leaves) to remove moisture and purify it.
الإهاب (al-ihab): Hide or skin of an animal; a material that can be purified through tanning.
الميتة (al-maytah): A dead animal that has not been slaughtered according to Islamic rites; its hide can be purified by tanning.
القرظ (al-qaraz): A tanning agent made from leaves of the acacia tree (salam).
التتريب (al-tatrib): Covering the hide with soil or dust as a form of figurative tanning.
التشميس (al-tashmis): Exposing the hide to the sun as a form of figurative tanning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Hide of a dead dog
C) Hide of a dead pig
D) Hide of a dead elephant

Key Takeaway
Tanning transforms an impure hide into a pure one, except for pig hide (intrinsically impure) and human skin (due to honor).

Purification of Hides by Tanning: Hanafi Ruling and Proofs
Ruling on purification of hides with hadith proofs and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purification of dead animal hides by tanning, identify the exceptions, and cite the primary hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Tanning purifies the hide of any dead animal except pig and human.

Tanning is not merely a manufacturing process; in Islamic law, it is a means of purification. The Hanafi school holds that real or virtual tanning renders the hide of a dead animal legally pure (tahir), allowing its use in prayer and ablution. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its evidence from the Sunnah, and the critical exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules that tanning (dibaghah) purifies the hide of a dead animal (maytah). Determine whether this is a ruling of purity (tahara) or permissibility.
The text explicitly states 'yathhar' (becomes pure).
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites multiple hadiths. Identify each hadith and note the key wording that indicates purification.
Key phrase: 'ayyama ihab dubbigh faqad tuhir'.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how each hadith proves that tanning causes purity. Focus on the imperative and declarative language.
The word 'tuhir' is explicit.
4
Note the exceptions
The passage excludes pig and human hides. Explain why: pig is najis al-ayn, human due to sanctity.
Other schools may include dog, but Hanafi permits dog hide.
5
Apply the ruling
Consider a practical scenario: a leather water bag made from a dead cow hide. After tanning, can it be used for wudu? Yes, according to Hanafi.
This differs from schools that do not accept tanning as purifying.

Section on the purification of hides of dead animals and similar. The hide of a dead animal becomes pure by real tanning, such as with qaraz (bark), and by virtual tanning, such as exposure to earth and sun, except the hide of the pig and the human. (The hide of a dead animal becomes pure), even if it is an elephant, because it is like other predatory animals in the soundest opinion, because he used to comb with a comb of ivory, which is elephant bone; and the hide of a dead dog becomes pure because it is not intrinsically impure. (And virtual tanning: like exposure to earth and sun) and leaving in the air; thus prayer and ablution are permissible in it and on it, due to his statement: 'Any hide that is tanned has become pure.' Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah, a commentary on Nur al-Idah, discusses the ruling on the purification of hides from dead animals (maytah). The author states that tanning—both real (using astringent substances) and virtual (by exposure to sun, air, or earth)—purifies the hide. This ruling applies to all animals, including elephants and dogs (since dogs are not intrinsically impure in the Hanafi school). The proof is multiple hadiths: the general statement 'Any hide that is tanned has become pure,' the specific incident of the Prophet's waterskin, and the instruction regarding the sheep of Maymunah. Exceptions are the pig (najis al-ayn, intrinsically impure) and the human (due to dignity; even if technically purified, use is prohibited). The commentary clarifies that tanning works by removing moisture from a hide that is originally pure; for the pig, since the hide itself is impure, tanning is ineffective. For humans, even if purity is achieved, the sanctity prevents use.

Key Terms
دباغة (dibaghah): Tanning; the process of treating animal hides with astringent substances or exposure to purify them.
إهاب (ihab): The hide or skin of an animal, specifically before removal of hair? Here it means the untanned skin.
ميتة (maytah): A dead animal that died without proper slaughter.
قرظ (qaraz): The leaves of the salam tree or fruit of the sant tree used for tanning; also referred to as 'acacia' bark.
نجس العين (najis al-ayn): Intrinsically impure; an object whose physical substance is considered impurity and cannot be purified.

Taharah — Purification

Purification by Slaughter and Parts Unaffected by Death
Ruling with proof on slaughter purification and unaffected parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of legal slaughter on the hide and meat of non-edible animals according to the mufta bihi, and identify which parts of a dead animal are not rendered impure.

Core Idea
Slaughter purifies the hide, not the meat; parts without flowing blood remain pure.

This lesson addresses two common scenarios: using hides from animals whose meat is impermissible, and handling parts like hair and bones from a dead animal. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the effect of slaughter on different parts, and between parts that contain blood and those that do not. You will learn the relied-upon position and the evidence from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage covers (a) the effect of legal slaughter on the hide and meat of non-edible animals, and (b) which parts of a dead animal do not become impure. Note the mufta bihi for each.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
Two opinions exist: al-Karkhi and others say slaughter purifies all parts; al-Hinduwani, al-Dabusi, and many mashayikh say only the hide is purified. The text sides with the latter as 'asahh ma yufta bihi'.
The dispute is based on whether purity is physical (blood removal) or legal (a shar'i ruling).
3
Analyze the evidence for the mufta bihi
The hadith from al-Daraqutni states the prohibition is only on meat, not hide, hair, or wool. Also Quran 6:145 implies meat is the primary forbidden item. The barrier between flesh and hide is cited as a legal consideration.
The hadith is ahad, thus zanni al-thubut, but its indication (dalala) is clear.
4
Apply the second ruling on parts without blood
Parts like hair, shorn wool, horns, hooves, and bones (if free of grease) are not made impure by death because they have no flowing blood. Only the attached end of plucked wool is impure.
Grease (dasm) on bones is impure; removing it restores purity of the bone.
5
Distinguish plucked vs shorn wool
Mansul (plucked) wool has an end that was attached to the hide, which is impure if the hide is impure; majzuz (shorn) wool is entirely separate and pure.
This distinction matters for practical use of wool from dead animals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وكل شيء) من أجزاء الحيوان غير الخنزير، (لا يسري فيه الدم لا ينجس بالموت)، لأن نجاسته باحتباس الدم، وهو منعدم في الأشياء التي هي (كالشعر، والريش المجزوز)، لأن المنسول طرفه المتصل بالجلد نجس، والقرن، والحافر، والعظم ما لم يكن به) أي: العظم (دسم)، أي: ودك، لأنه نجس من الميتة، فإذا زال عن العظم زال عنه النجس، لأن العظم في ذاته طاهر فيكون متنجساً بالدسم؛ لما أخرج الدارقطني: إنما حرم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من الميتة لحمها، فأما الجلد والشعر والصوف، فلا بأس به، وقال: (لا أجد في ما أوحي إلي محرماً على طاعم يطعمه) الأنعام: 145، الآية، ألا كل شيء من الميتة حلال إلا ما أكل منها، فأما الجلد والقرن
And the legal slaughter purifies the hide of a non-edible animal, but not its meat, according to the soundest fatwa. And everything in which blood does not flow does not become impure by death, such as hair, plucked feathers, horn, hoof, and bone unless it has grease. (And the legal slaughter purifies) excludes slaughter by a Magian, by a pilgrim in ihram hunting, and one who intentionally omits the basmalah, (the hide of a non-edible animal) other than the pig and the dog, according to the view of its intrinsic impurity; because the action of slaughter is like the action of tanning in removing impure moisture, rather it is more effective, because it prevents its connection to the hide, while tanning removes after connection due to the corruption of the structure by death. As for before (slaughter), everything created in its place, and a barrier is placed between the flesh and the hide, just as a barrier is placed between blood and milk until it emerges pure. (But not its meat) so its meat remains impure. (According to the soundest fatwa of two differing corrections regarding the purity of the meat of non-edible animals and its fat by legal slaughter. Al-Karkhi said: Every animal is purified by slaughter, so this indicates that its meat, hide, and all its parts are purified, because the name 'animal' refers to the totality of parts. This was corrected by the author of al-Hidayah and al-Tuhfah, and in al-Bada'i it is closer to correctness because impurity is due to poured blood, and it has been removed by slaughter. Al-Hinduwani, Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, and many mashayikh said: it purifies its hide, but not its meat and fat, and the author of al-Nihayah chose this, because purity and its opposite are legal considerations only; there is evidence of purity of a slaughtered animal whose blood did not flow for a reason, and impurity of a slaughtered animal by a Magian even if its blood flows. So the consideration of purity of the hide without the meat is legally due to the barrier placed in the slaughterer, or due to need to benefit from it but not from its meat, since there is nothing to make it lawful legally. The legal causes have their effects; here permissibility is the original ruling, and purity is a consequence. (And everything) from the parts of an animal other than the pig, (in which blood does not flow does not become impure by death), because its impurity is due to the retention of blood, and this is absent in things like (hair, plucked feathers), because the plucked part — its end attached to the hide is impure; and horn, hoof, and bone unless it has (dasm) i.e. grease, because it is impure from the dead animal; so when it is removed from the bone, the impurity is removed from it, because the bone in itself is pure, so it becomes impure by the grease; because al-Daraqutni narrated: The Messenger of Allah only forbade from the dead animal its meat; as for the hide, hair, and wool, there is no harm in them. And he said: 'I do not find in what was revealed to me anything forbidden to one who eats it' (al-An'am 145), meaning: everything from the dead animal is lawful except what is eaten from it; as for the hide and horn...
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage discusses two connected rulings: (1) The effect of legal slaughter (al-dhakiyah al-shar'iyyah) on the hide of animals whose meat is not eaten (e.g., predatory animals). The relied-upon opinion (mufta bihi) is that slaughter purifies the hide (making it permissible to use) but does not purify the meat, which remains najis. The text presents an internal Hanafi dispute: al-Karkhi and the authors of al-Hidayah, al-Tuhfah, and al-Bada'i hold that slaughter purifies all parts, arguing that the animal is a single entity and impurity is from poured blood which slaughter removes. Al-Hinduwani, al-Dabusi, and many others hold that only the hide is purified, based on a legal distinction: purity/impurity is a legal ruling, and evidence shows that a slaughtered animal becomes pure only in hide due to a barrier between flesh and hide (similar to the barrier between blood and milk). The text sides with the latter as 'asahh ma yufta bihi'. (2) Parts of a dead animal that do not contain flowing blood (hair, wool, horns, hooves, bones if free from grease) are not rendered impure by death. The impurity of dead animals is due to trapped blood; these parts have no blood flow. The evidence is the hadith that only the meat is forbidden, and the hide, hair, and wool are permissible. Bones are pure inherently but become impure if they have grease; removal of grease restores purity.

Key Terms
ذكاة شرعية (dhakiyyah shar'iyyah): Legal slaughter performed according to Islamic requirements (proper cutting of throat, basmalah, etc.).
غير المأكول (ghayr al-ma'kul): Animals whose meat is not permitted to eat, such as predators.
دسم (dasm): Grease or fat that may be attached to bones; it is impure from a dead animal.
منسول (mansul): Plucked wool or hair; the part attached to the hide is impure, but the detached part is pure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon opinion) in the text, what does legal slaughter do to the hide and meat of a non-edible animal?
A) It purifies both the hide and the meat
B) It purifies the hide but not the meat
C) It purifies neither the hide nor the meat
D) It purifies the meat but not the hide

Key Takeaway
Legal slaughter purifies the hide of non-edible animals; parts without flowing blood are inherently pure and unaffected by death.

Tazkiyah Purifies the Skin of Non-Edible Animals but Not the Meat
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of purification by tazkiyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of tazkiyah on non-edible animals, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Tazkiyah purifies the skin of non-edible animals, not the meat — purity is a legal ruling, not merely a physical consequence.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi dispute about the scope of purification (taharah) achieved through Islamic ritual slaughter (tazkiyah) for animals whose meat is not permissible to eat. The matn states that only the skin is purified, but this was contested by some early Hanafi authorities. We will explore the reasoning on both sides and understand why the majority of later scholars adopted the view that the meat remains impure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling in the Matn
Read the base text: tazkiyah purifies the skin of non-edible animals, but not the meat. Note that this is the ruling that the commentary expands upon.
The matn is concise; the commentary provides the details of the dispute.
2
Understand the Analogy to Tanning
The commentary explains that tazkiyah acts like tanning (dibaghah) in removing impure moistures, but is even more effective because it prevents attachment of impurities before death. This analogy supports why the skin becomes pure.
The barrier between skin and meat is highlighted.
3
Present the Two Hanafi Positions
Identify the two views: (1) Imam al-Karakhi and others hold that all parts are purified. (2) Al-Hinduwani, Abu Zaid al-Dabusi, and many mashayikh hold that only the skin is purified. Note the evidence each side offers.
The first view relies on physical removal of blood; the second views purity as a legal ruling.
4
Analyze the Tarjih (Preference)
The author of al-Nihayah and many later scholars preferred the second view. They argued that purity is a legal consideration not reducible to blood removal, citing counterexamples like slaughter without blood flow and Magian slaughter. Also, practical need for skin and a barrier between skin and meat support the distinction.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتُطَهِّرُ الزَّكاةُ الشّرعيّةُ) خرج بها ذبح المجوسي شيئاً، والمحرم صيداً، وتارك التسمية عمداً)، (جِلْدَ غَيرِ المَأكُولِ) سوى الخنزير والكلب، على القول بنجاسة عينه؛ لعمل الذكـــاة عـمــل الدباغة في إزالة الرطوبات النجسة، بل أولى، لأنها تمنع من اتصالها بـه، والدباغة تزيـل بـعـد الاتصال لفساد البنية بالموت، فأما قبله، فكل شيء مخلوق فيه بمحله، وجعل بين اللحم والجلد حاجزاً، كما جعل بين الدم واللبن حاجزاً حتى خرج طاهراً ( دُونَ لَحْمِهِ) فيبقى لحمه نجساً، (عَلَى أصَحَ مَا يُفتَى بِهِ من التصحيحين المختلفين في طهارة لحم غير المأكول وشحمه بالذكاة الشرعية. قال « الكرخي » : كل حيوان يطهر بالذكاة، فهذا يدل على أنه يطهر لحمه وجلده وسائر أجزائه، لأن الحيوان اسم لجملة الأجزاء، وصححه صاحب « الهداية »، و « التحفة » ، وفي «البدائع » أنه أقرب للصواب. لأن النجاسة بالدم المسفوح ، وقد زال بالذكاة. وقال « الهندواني» و«أبو زيد الدبوسي» وكثير من المشايخ يطهر جلده لا لحمه وشحمه واختاره صاحب ( النهاية»، لأن الطهارة وضدها اعتبار شرعي ليس إلا بدليل طهارة مذبوح لم يسل دمه لعارض، ونجاسة مذبوح مجوسي وإن سال دمه، فاعتبار طهارة الجلد دون اللحم شرعاً للحاجز المفروض في المذكي، أو للاحتياج إليه، انتفاعاً به دون لحمه، لعدم ما يسيغه شرعاً، إذ الأسباب الشرعية بمسبباتها، والحل هنا هو الحكم الأصلي، والطهارة من لوازمه.
And (the word 'al-zakah') here means Islamic ritual slaughter (tazkiyah) – excludes slaughter by a Magian, a muhrim (in ihram) hunting, and one who intentionally omits tasmiyah – purifies the skin of non-edible animals, except pig and dog according to the opinion of their intrinsic impurity; because the action of tazkiyah is like the action of tanning in removing impure moistures, rather it is more effective, because it prevents their attachment to it, while tanning removes them after attachment due to corruption of structure by death. Before [death], everything is created in its place, and a barrier is placed between meat and skin, just as a barrier is placed between blood and milk until it exits pure. (Without its meat) so its meat remains impure, (according to the most correct of what is given as fatwa) from the two correct positions differing on the purity of the meat and fat of non-edible animals by Islamic slaughter. Al-Karakhi said: Every animal is purified by tazkiyah, so this indicates that it purifies its meat, skin, and all its parts, because the term animal applies to the whole of its parts. The author of al-Hidayah and al-Tuhfah affirmed this, and in al-Bada'i' it is said to be closer to correctness, because impurity is due to poured-out blood, and that has been removed by tazkiyah. Al-Hinduwani, Abu Zaid al-Dabusi, and many mashayikh said: It purifies its skin, not its meat and fat. The author of al-Nihayah chose this, because purity and its opposite are purely legal considerations, based on the evidence of purity of an animal slaughtered but whose blood did not flow due to some impediment, and impurity of a slaughtered animal by a Magian even if its blood flowed. So legally considering the purification of the skin without the meat is due to the assumed barrier in the slaughtered animal, or due to the need to benefit from it without its meat, since there is no legal way to consume it. For legal causes are linked to their effects, and here lawful consumption is the original ruling, and purity is one of its necessary concomitants.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Nur al-Idah discusses the effect of Islamic ritual slaughter (tazkiyah) on non-edible animals (ghayr al-ma'kul). The ruling stated in the matn is that tazkiyah purifies the skin of such animals but not the meat, and this is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). The commentator expands by clarifying that tazkiyah acts like tanning (dibaghah) in removing impure moisture, but it is even more effective because it prevents the attachment of impurities, whereas tanning removes them after they have already attached due to the corruption of the dead body. The text then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam al-Karakhi held that all parts of the animal are purified by tazkiyah, because the term 'animal' encompasses all parts, and impurity is caused by spilled blood, which is removed by tazkiyah. This view was supported by the authors of al-Hidayah, al-Tuhfah, and al-Bada'i'. On the other hand, al-Hinduwani, Abu Zaid al-Dabusi, and many other mashayikh maintained that only the skin is purified, not the meat or fat. They argued that purity and impurity are legal rulings subject to textual evidence, not pure rational deduction. They pointed to cases where a slaughtered animal's blood does not flow due to an impediment yet remains pure, and the slaughter of a Magian which is impure even if blood flows. Therefore, the purification of the skin alone is established by the Shari'ah due to a barrier between skin and meat, and the practical need to benefit from the skin without consuming the meat. The commentator concludes by stating that the legal cause (sabab) of tazkiyah yields its effects, and lawful consumption is the original ruling, with purity remaining as a necessary concomitant.

Key Terms
الذكاة (al-dhakāh): Islamic ritual slaughter that makes the animal permissible to eat and purifies its skin.
الدباغة (al-dibāghah): Tanning of skin using chemicals, which purifies the skin of a dead animal.
المذكي (al-mudhakki): The person who performs the ritual slaughter (tazkiyah).
غير المأكول (ghayr al-ma'kūl): Animals whose meat is not permissible to eat, such as predators.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what does tazkiyah (Islamic slaughter) purify in a non-edible animal?
A) Both the skin and the meat
B) Only the skin
C) Only the meat
D) Neither the skin nor the meat

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, tazkiyah purifies the skin of non-edible animals but not the meat, as this is the most carefully relied-upon position.

Purity of Animal Parts Without Blood Flow
Ruling with proof from hadith and internal ikhtilaf on nerves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which animal parts remain pure after death according to the Hanafi school, apply the dalil from hadith, and identify the exception for nerves.

Core Idea
Parts without blood flow (hair, horn, hoof, bone without marrow) are pure; nerves are impure because they contain life.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi taharah: the death of an animal does not automatically render all its parts impure. The determining factor is the presence or absence of blood circulation. We will study the ruling, its evidence from hadith, and an important internal difference on the status of nerves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and its scope
Read the first sentence of the source: 'Everything in which blood does not flow is not made impure by death.' Note the examples: hair, plucked feathers, horn, hoof, and bone without marrow.
The ruling applies to any animal, not only those slaughtered Islamically, as long as the part lacks blood circulation.
2
Understand the underlying cause (illah)
The text explains that impurity in a dead animal arises from retained blood. Parts without blood flow lack this cause, thus remain pure. This is a clear application of qiyas.
The illah is 'retention of blood' (ihtibas al-dam).
3
Analyze the hadith proof
Al-Daraqutni's hadith explicitly excludes skin, hair, and wool from the prohibition. The commentator uses this to support the ruling for analogous parts like horn and hoof.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted in the school.
4
Examine the exception: bone with marrow
Bone itself is pure, but if it contains fatty marrow (dasm), the marrow is impure and contaminates the bone. Once the marrow is removed, the bone becomes pure.
This distinguishes between the bone substance and the marrow; only the latter is najis.
5
Resolve the internal disagreement on nerves
The text presents two views: (a) nerves are impure because they contain life (pain when cut), and (b) nerves are pure like non-hardened bone. The correct (sahih) view is that nerves are impure.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the mufta bihi is that nerves are najis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل شيء لا يسري فيه الدم لا ينجس بالموتِ، كالشعر، والريش المجزوز، والقرن، والحافر، والعظم ما لم يكن به دَسَمٌ)، لأن نجاسته باحتباس الدم، وهو منعدم في الأشياء التي هي (كالشعر، والريش المجزوز)، لأن المنسول طرفه المتصل بالجلد نجس، والقَرْنِ، والحافر، والعظم ما لم يكن به) أي: العظم (دَسَمٌ)، أي: ودك، لأنه نجس من الميتة، فإذا زال عن العظم زال عنه النجس، لأن العظم في ذاته طاهر فيكون متنجساً بالدسم؛ لما أخرج الدر اقطني : إِنَّمَا حَرَّمَ رسولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مِن الميتة لحمها، فأما الجلد والشعر والصوف، فلا بأس به ، (٣)، وقال : ( لا أَجِدُ فِي مَا أُوحِيَ إِلَى مُحَرَّمًا عَلَى طَاعِمٍ يطعمه الانقل: ١٤٥]، الآية، ألا كلُّ شيءٍ مِنَ الميئةِ حَلالٌ إلا مَا أكَلَ مِنْهَا، فَأَمَّا الجلد والقرن والعَصَبُ نَجِس، في الصحيح) من الرواية، لأن فيه حياة، بدليل التألم بقطعه، وقيل: طاهر، لأنه عظم غير متصلب.
And everything in which blood does not flow is not made impure by death, such as hair, plucked feathers, horn, hoof, and bone as long as it has no fat (marrow). Because its impurity is due to the retention of blood, and that is absent in things like hair and plucked feathers (since the plucked part that was attached to the skin is impure), horn, hoof, and bone as long as it has no fat, because fat is impure from the dead animal, so if it is removed from the bone, the impurity is removed from the bone, because the bone itself is pure and becomes contaminated by the fat. Due to what al-Daraqutni narrated: 'Indeed, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade from the dead animal only its meat; as for the skin, hair, and wool, there is no harm.' And he said: 'I do not find in what has been revealed to me anything forbidden to one who eats it... except that everything from the dead animal is lawful except what is eaten of it; as for the skin, horn, and nerve, they are impure according to the correct (narration).' From the narration, because there is life in it, evidenced by the pain felt when it is cut; and it is said: it is pure because it is a non-hardened bone.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage addresses the purity of animal parts after death without ritual slaughter. The ruling states that any part of an animal in which blood does not circulate—such as hair, wool, horn, hoof, and bone without marrow—is not rendered impure by the animal's death. The reason is that impurity in dead animals is caused by the retention of blood, and these parts have no blood flowing within them. However, if a bone contains fatty marrow (dasm), the marrow is impure and contaminates the bone; once the marrow is removed, the bone becomes pure. The commentator supports this with a hadith from al-Daraqutni: the Prophet (peace be upon him) said that only the meat of a dead animal is forbidden, not the skin, hair, or wool. Another narration is cited from the Quran (Surah al-An'am, 6:145) indicating that everything from a dead animal is lawful except its meat, but then adds that skin, horn, and nerve are impure. The text then addresses the nerve (asab): according to the correct narration, it is impure because it contains life—a nerve causes pain when cut. There is also a view that it is pure because it resembles a non-hardened bone. The relied-upon position is that the nerve is impure.

Key Terms
لا يسري فيه الدم (lā yasrī fīhi al-dam): Parts of the body in which blood does not flow or circulate, such as hair, horn, etc.
المجزوز (al-majzūz): Plucked or cut off, referring to feathers or hair that have been removed.
العصب (al-ʿaṣab): The nerve; a fibrous tissue that conducts signals and causes pain when cut.
الدسم (al-dasm): Fat or marrow within bone; considered impure from a dead animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is pure but cannot be used in prayer

Key Takeaway
The determinative factor for impurity after death is blood circulation; parts without it remain pure, except for nerves due to the presence of life.

Purity of Musk and Civet: The Principle of Istihalah
Ruling with proof based on istihalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the purity of musk and civet, explain the reasoning based on istihalah, and apply the principle of transformation to determine purity of other substances.

Core Idea
Transformation (istihalah) purifies an impure substance, as seen in musk and civet.

This passage concludes the chapter on impurities by addressing two valuable aromatic substances: musk and civet. You will learn that despite their origin from impure sources (blood and secretion), they are ruled pure due to a complete transformation into a pleasant substance. This introduces you to the important Hanafi principle of istihalah, which can purify certain materials.

Guided Path
1
Identify the substances and their rulings
Read the passage and list the two substances mentioned (musk and civet) and the specific ruling given for each: the musk pod is pure, musk itself is pure and halal to eat, civet is pure, and prayer while perfumed with them is valid.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The passage states that the purity is due to istihalah (transformation) into a pleasant substance. For musk, it is originally blood of the musk deer, yet it becomes pure. The text mentions consensus (ittifaq) on musk's purity.
The proof here is rational (dalil aqli): transformation changes the essence.
3
Understand the principle of istihalah
Istihalah is the complete change of a substance from one state to another, such that it no longer retains its original properties. Hanafi fiqh accepts istihalah as a purifier. Reflect on how this applies to musk (blood to musk) and civet (secretion to perfume).
Compare with other schools that may require additional conditions or not accept istihalah.
4
Consider the consensus and its implication
The text says 'there is agreement (ittifaq) on its purity, only due to transformation'. This indicates that the ruling is well-established in the Hanafi school and not disputed. This strengthens the application of the principle.
Civet follows the same reasoning by analogy.
5
Apply to similar cases
Think of other substances that undergo transformation, such as wine turning to vinegar, or bones turning to fertilizer. How would the principle of istihalah apply? The ruling would be that the new substance is pure if the transformation is complete.
This step helps you extend the learning beyond the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونافجة المسك طاهرة، كالمسك، وأكله حلال، والزباد طاهر، تصح صلاة متطيب به، لاستحالته إلى الطيبية، كالمسك فإنه بعض دم الغزال، وقد اتفق على طهارته وليس إلا بالاستحالة للطيبية، والاستحالة مطهرة والله الموفق بمنه وكرمه وعونه.
And the musk pod (nafijat al-misk) is pure, like musk itself, and eating it is lawful; civet (zabad) is pure, and the prayer of one who perfumes himself with it is valid, because of its transformation (istihalah) into a pleasant substance, like musk, which is some blood of the musk deer, and there is consensus on its purity, only due to transformation into a pleasant substance, and transformation (istihalah) is a purifier. And Allah is the granter of success, by His grace and generosity and help.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This short passage concludes the section on impurities and purifications by addressing two specific substances: musk and civet (zabad). The ruling is that the musk pod (the sac containing musk from the musk deer) is pure, musk itself is pure, and eating musk is permissible (halal). Similarly, civet (a secretion from the civet cat used in perfumery) is pure, and a person's prayer is valid if they have perfumed themselves with it. The reasoning provided is the principle of istihalah (transformation): these substances originate from impure sources (musk is originally blood from the musk deer; civet is a secretion), but they undergo a change into a pleasant, aromatic substance. This transformation purifies them. The commentator notes that there is consensus (ittifaq) on the purity of musk, implying that the same principle applies to civet. The principle 'al-istihalah mutahhirah' (transformation is a purifier) is well-known in Hanafi fiqh. Thus, musk and civet are treated as pure substances, and one may use them in prayer and even consume musk as a food or medicine, provided it is not harmful.

Key Terms
نافجة المسك (nāfijat al-misk): The pod or sac in the musk deer that contains the musk substance.
المسك (al-misk): Musk, a fragrant substance derived from the musk deer; considered pure and halal.
الزباد (al-zabād): Civet, a musky secretion from the civet cat used in perfumery; ruled pure.
الاستحالة (al-istihālah): Transformation of a substance from one state to another; a method of purification in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Musk and civet are pure because they undergo istihalah (transformation), a process accepted in Hanafi fiqh as a means of purification.

Ruling on Late-Night Conversation (Samar) After Isha
Discussing the permissibility of staying awake after Isha for conversation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on samar after Isha and distinguish between permissible and disliked forms based on the evidence.

Core Idea
Late-night conversation is not inherently sinful; its ruling depends on its purpose and effect on worship.

The passage addresses the ruling on staying awake after Isha prayer for conversation, known as samar. It presents a scholarly difference: some absolutely prohibit it, while others, including the Hanafi school, permit it if the conversation is for a religious benefit such as studying knowledge. The author cites evidence from the Prophet's practice and hadith, explaining that the reported disapproval refers to samar that leads to missing Fajr or idle talk.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is samar: staying awake after Isha for conversation. The Hanafi ruling is makruh tanzih (disliked but not prohibited) unless the conversation is for a religious benefit.
The ruling is not absolute; it varies with intention and content.
2
Locate the evidence
The passage presents two sets of evidence: a hadith that the Prophet forbade samar except for a praying person or a traveler (indicating prohibition), and a narration from Umar that the Prophet himself engaged in samar with companions (indicating permissibility).
The conflict is resolved by interpreting the prohibition as specific to idle or harmful talk.
3
Interpret the evidence
The Hanafi school reconciles the evidence by understanding the prohibition as referring to samar that causes one to miss Fajr prayer or involves vain talk. The permissive evidence shows that samar for good purposes (e.g., religious study) is allowed.
Al-Tahawi notes that sleep before Isha is only disliked for those who fear missing prayer.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars hold samar as absolutely haram, citing the general prohibition. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on purpose, following the principle that acts are judged by intentions.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf grounded in different interpretations of the same evidence.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that samar is allowed for religious benefit (e.g., studying sacred knowledge, discussing beneficial matters) and disliked if it is idle or leads to neglect of worship.
This position is consistent with the general Hanafi emphasis on intention (niyyah).

(٤) أخرجه البيهقي في السنن الكبرى (٤٥٢/١)، وعبد الــ العشاء إلا لمصل أو مسافر».
Warning: We pointed to mentioning samar, so we mention it, and mention before it, and speech before Fajr prayer and after it. He said in al-Muhsin: The scholars differed regarding the permissibility of samar after Isha absolutely, and some permitted it if it is for good and discussion of knowledge. If it was permissible before Isha, they did not mention disapproval. It is narrated from Umar: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to engage in samar with...' (2) Narrated by Ahmad... (3) Narrated by al-Bukhari... (4) Narrated by al-Bayhaqi... except for a praying person or a traveler.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage from the sharh examines the ruling on samar—staying awake after Isha prayer for conversation. The author notes a difference of opinion among scholars: some absolutely forbid it, while others permit it if the conversation is for a good purpose such as religious study or discussing beneficial knowledge. The text cites a hadith from Umar that the Prophet (peace be upon him) himself engaged in samar, indicating permissibility in some contexts. The reported disapproval is interpreted as referring to samar that causes one to miss the dawn prayer or leads to idle talk. The author also mentions that talking before Fajr prayer is disliked. Al-Tahawi explains that the prohibition of sleeping before Isha is for those who fear missing the prayer. The passage concludes that samar is prohibited based on a hadith, but the prohibition is not absolute; it is mitigated by the purpose of the conversation. The Hanafi position is that samar is disliked (makruh) unless for a religious benefit.

Key Terms
سمر (samar): Late-night conversation after the Isha prayer.

D) It is an innovation (bid'ah)

Key Takeaway
Late-night conversation after Isha is generally disliked but becomes permissible and even commendable if pursued for religious benefit.

The Three Disliked Times: Hanafi Ruling on Invalidity of Fard/Wajib Prayers
Defines three disliked times and rules fard/wajib prayers invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three disliked times, explain why fard/wajib prayers are invalid during them, and defend the Hanafi position against objections using hadith and analogy.

Core Idea
A fard prayer owed in the dhimmah is invalid at sunrise, zenith, and sunset because a deficient act cannot fulfill a complete obligation.

The Prophet ﷺ forbade prayer at three specific times of the day. But does that prohibition make the prayer invalid or merely disliked? In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling that any fard or wajib prayer performed at these times is entirely void. We will trace the proof from the hadith, define the exact times, and see how Hanafi scholars reconcile apparent conflicts with other texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three disliked times
Locate the three periods: sunrise until it rises (approx. a spear's length), zenith until declination, and yellowness until sunset. Note the precise definitions from al-Asl and other sources.
The exact duration of the first period is defined by the ability to look at the sun's disk.
2
State the ruling and its scope
The passage rules that 'nothing of the obligatory prayers and duties that have become due in the dhimmah are valid' during these times. Identify that this covers fard and wajib prayers owed before their time (qada) and also those performed within their time? Actually, the text says 'before their entry'? Actually, it says 'before their entry' meaning prayers that have become due but not yet performed? The passage clarifies it includes prayers that are already owed (lazimat fi al-dhimmah).
The ruling applies to fard and wajib prayers, not to nafl (supererogatory) prayers.
3
Analyze the primary dalil
Read the hadith of Uqbah ibn Amir: three times the Prophet forbade prayer and burial of dead. Understand that 'burial' alludes to funeral prayer. The prohibition indicates deficiency in the pillars (arkan), so a deficient act cannot fulfill a complete obligation.
The Hanafi use of this hadith: prohibition implies invalidity because the act is deficient in arkan.
4
Examine the conflict and resolution
Other hadiths command making up missed prayers at any time and allow catching a rak'ah before sunrise/sunset. The Hanafi response: these conflict with the prohibition, so analogy is used. For Fajr, the prohibition prevails (invalid); for Asr, the permissive hadith prevails (valid). Alternatively, al-Tahawi held the prohibition abrogates the general permission.
This reconciliation shows the Hanafi method of ta'arud (conflict) and tarjih (preference).

Chapter on the disliked times. Three times in which nothing of the obligatory prayers and the duties that have become due in the dhimmah are valid before their entry: at sunrise until it rises, at its zenith until it declines, and at its yellowness until it sets. Chapter on the disliked times: Three times in which nothing of the obligatory prayers and duties that have become due in the dhimmah are valid before their entry ( ) i.e., the disliked times: first: at sunrise until it rises and becomes white. He said in al-Asl: When the sun rises a spear or two spears, prayer becomes permissible. Al-Fadli said: As long as a person is able to look at the disk of the sun, the sun is rising, so prayer is not permitted therein; when he is unable to look, it becomes permitted. Abu Hafs al-Sufkardari said: A basin is placed on level ground; as long as the sun's rays fall on its walls, it is rising; when they fall only in its center, it has risen and prayer becomes permissible. So in al-Mi'raj. And the second: at its zenith (i.e., in the middle of the sky) until it declines (i.e., tilts toward the west). And the third: at its yellowness and weakness until the eye can face it, until it sets, due to the saying of Uqbah ibn Amir: Three times the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, forbade us to pray in and to bury our dead in: at sunrise until it rises, at its zenith until it declines, and when it inclines for setting until it sets. Narrated by Muslim and others. The meaning of 'to bury our dead' is the funeral prayer, for burying is not disliked, but it is alluded to due to the connection between them. And it has come explicitly in the Book of Funerals of Imam Abu Hafs Umar ibn Shahin: 'He forbade us to pray over our dead at three: at sunrise... etc.' And in al-Muwatta' and al-Nasa'i, he said: 'Indeed the sun rises between the two horns of Satan, and when it rises he separates from it, then when it is at its zenith he accompanies it, then when it declines he separates from it, then when it inclines for setting he accompanies it, and when it sets he separates from it.' And he forbade prayer in those hours. The intended meaning is the funeral that occurred before these times, as we will mention. And the reason for invalidity is adherence to the performed act: the establishment of dislike in the act. And what is due in the dhimmah as a complete act is not fulfilled by a deficient one. And the prohibition of legal acts, although it affirms legality, implies validity with dislike if done, but we say here it is invalid absolutely, due to a deficiency in the pillars, so what is due completely is not fulfilled by it. And it cannot be said that this is reasoning in opposition to the text. And that is His saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it' - agreed upon. And it is general for all times. And His saying: 'Whoever catches a rak'ah of the morning prayer before sunrise has caught the morning prayer, and whoever catches a rak'ah of the afternoon prayer before sunset has caught the afternoon prayer' - narrated by al-Shaykhan and al-Tahawi. And His saying: 'And when one of you catches a prostration of the afternoon prayer before sunset, let him complete his prayer; and when he catches a prostration of the morning prayer before sunrise, let him complete his prayer' - narrated by al-Bukhari. For we say: when conflict occurred between this and the hadith forbidding prayer in the three times, we revert to analogy as is the rule of conflict. So the ruling of this hadith prevailed for the validity of the afternoon prayer, and the ruling of the prohibition prevailed for the morning prayer, so it is not valid. And preferring the prohibitive over the permissive is only when there is no analogy; when there is, preference is for it. And Ibn Umar narrated that he, peace be upon him, said: 'When the sun rises then refrain from prayer, for it rises between two horns of Satan' - narrated by Muslim. And he also narrated: 'And the time of the morning prayer is from dawn until the sun rises; when the sun rises, then refrain from prayer.' Moreover, it is mentioned in al-Asrar that the prohibition is later, because it always supervenes on the established original, and because the Companions acted upon it, so it is known that it is subsequent. Rather, al-Tahawi said: They are all abrogated by the prohibitive texts; otherwise, it would necessitate acting upon part of the hadith and leaving part merely by our saying that a deficient act supervened on a complete one in Fajr, unlike the Asr of the same day, even though deficiency accompanied Asr from the beginning and accompanied Fajr in duration; so it would be invalid in Asr as in Fajr.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It begins by stating the three times when prayer is disliked and invalid: at sunrise until it rises, at midday until it declines, and at sunset until it sets. The author clarifies the ruling: no fard (obligatory) or wajib (necessary) prayers that are owed in the dhimmah are valid if performed during these times. The passage then defines each time precisely: for sunrise, different opinions are given about when it ends (a spear's length, or until the disk can be looked at, or when shadow falls in the center of a basin). The proof given is the hadith of Uqbah ibn Amir forbidding prayer and burial of dead in these times; 'burial of dead' is interpreted as funeral prayer. Another hadith explains that the sun rises between the horns of Satan, and prayer is forbidden at these times. The commentary then explains the Hanafi reasoning for invalidity: the prohibition indicates deficiency in the pillars of the prayer, so performing a deficient act cannot fulfill the obligation of a complete act. An objection is raised: general hadiths command making up missed prayers at any time and allowing completion of a rak'ah caught before sunrise/sunset. The Hanafi response resolves the conflict by reverting to analogy and preferring the prohibition for Fajr while allowing Asr based on the permissive hadith. Further, the author mentions the view of al-Tahawi that the prohibitory hadith abrogate the general permission, as they are later. This establishes the Hanafi position that these times render fard/wajib prayers invalid.

Key Terms
الذمة (adh-dhimmah): The legal liability or obligation; prayers that have become a debt upon the person
الفَرائِض (al-fara'id): Obligatory prayers (fard) that are a debt on the person
الوَاجِبَات (al-wajibat): Necessary prayers (wajib) such as witr and obligatory vows
الأَرْكَان (al-arkan): The essential pillars of prayer (e.g., standing, recitation, bowing, prostration) that define its validity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling for a person who prays the fard of Asr during the time when the sun is yellowing (beginning of sunset)?
A) The prayer is valid but disliked
B) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated
C) The prayer is valid and not disliked
D) The prayer is invalid only if performed intentionally

Key Takeaway
Fard and wajib prayers performed at sunrise, zenith, or sunset are invalid in the Hanafi school and must be repeated.
