And knowing the manner of prayer, and what it contains of obligatory qualities in a way that distinguishes them from recommended qualities, or believing that it is obligatory, so that one does not perform a supererogatory act with an obligatory act. Unlike reading, it is of no benefit. As for the final sitting, if one performs it while asleep, in 'Munyat al-Musalli': if he sleeps through the entire sitting, he must sit when he wakes up for the duration of the tashahhud, and if he does not sit, his prayer is invalidated. And it contradicts what is in 'Jami' al-Fatawa': that if he sits for the duration of the tashahhud while asleep, it is valid. And it is explained in 'al-Tahqiq' by Sheikh Abdul Aziz al-Bukhari: because it is not a pillar and its basis is rest, so sleep suits it. End. I say: This is the fruit of the dispute between those who say it is a condition and those who say it is a pillar: it is valid while asleep according to the view that it is a condition, not a pillar. And this is a response to the statement of the author of 'al-Bahr': 'They agreed on the obligatory nature of the final sitting and differed on its pillarhood, and I have not seen anyone who addressed the fruit of this difference.' End. So praise be to Allah. And for the validity of performing the obligatory prayer, one of two matters is required, the minimum by which the obligation is fulfilled. The first: knowing the manner of the prayer, and that is knowing the reality of what it contains of the obligatory qualities, meaning that it is obligatory, such as his belief that the two rak'ahs of Fajr are obligatory, and the four in Zuhr are obligatory, and that the sunnah of Fajr is two rak'ahs not obligatory, and the sunnah of Zuhr is four not obligatory, and so on for the rest of the prayers. The obligatory ones are such that he distinguishes them by what we mentioned. It is not a condition that he distinguish what the Fajr prayer and the like contain of obligatory and sunnah, such as that he believe that standing is a pillar, the thana' and ta'awwudh are sunnah, recitation is obligatory, and tasbih is sunnah. Rather, the intent is to distinguish the prayer in itself as being obligatory, so it is distinguished from the recommended qualities by believing that the rak'ahs he prays are sunnah aside from the obligatory, like the regular sunnah prayers and others. The second of the two matters is indicated by his statement: and the worshipper's belief that they are all obligatory, such as his belief that all four in Fajr are obligatory, and the five in Maghrib are obligatory, and he prays three rak'ahs of them separately. Because supererogatory prayer is valid with the intention of obligatory but not vice versa, and to this he pointed with his statement: so that he does not perform a supererogatory act with an obligatory act by believing that what he did is supererogatory. He said in 'al-Tajnis wa al-Mazid': A man who did not know that the five daily prayers are obligatory upon the servants, but he used to pray them (meaning without intention in their times) is not valid and he must make them up; because he did not intend the obligatory, and intention is a condition. Similarly, if he knows that some rak'ahs are obligatory and some are sunnah but does not distinguish the obligatory from the sunnah, it is not valid for what we said, unless he prays behind an imam and intends his prayer. And if he thinks that all are obligatory, his prayer suffices him, because the supererogatory is fulfilled by the intention of obligatory, whereas the obligatory is not fulfilled by the intention of supererogatory. And if he knows the obligatory from the supererogatory but does not know what is in the prayer (i.e., within the same prayer of obligatory and sunnah), his prayer is valid; because when he knows the obligatory, he intends the obligatory. End. And similar in: And the pillars from the mentioned are four: standing, recitation, bowing, and prostration. And it is said: the final sitting the extent of the tashahhud (is also a pillar). And the rest are conditions: some are conditions for the validity of beginning the prayer, which is what is outside it. And other is a condition for the continuity of its validity. Al-Khulasah. Then he said: If he leads a group while he does not know the obligatory prayer from the supererogatory prayer, and he intends the whole as obligatory, his prayer is valid. As for the prayer of the group, every prayer that has no sunnah before it, like 'Asr, Maghrib, and 'Isha, is permissible, and the prayer of the group is not permissible in every prayer that has a sunnah before it, like Fajr and Zuhr.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is a commentary on Nur al-Idah, dealing with the conditions for performing obligatory prayers validly. The commentator begins by stating the requirement that the worshipper must either know how to distinguish the obligatory elements (fard) from the recommended ones (sunnah) within his prayer, or believe that all the rak'ahs he is praying are obligatory. This is to prevent him from mistakenly performing a supererogatory prayer with the intention of obligatory, which would render it invalid. The commentator then digresses into a dispute about the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah). According to Munyat al-Musalli, if one sleeps through the entire sitting, he must sit up upon waking for the duration of the tashahhud; otherwise, the prayer is invalid. However, Jami' al-Fatawa holds that if he sits the duration while asleep, it is valid. The difference stems from whether the final sitting is a rukn (pillar) or a shart (condition). If it is a rukn, sleep invalidates it; if a shart, sleep does not harm because its basis is rest. The commentator (the author of Imdad al-Fattah) notes that this is the practical consequence (thamarah) of the ikhtilaf, which the author of al-Bahr had lamented not finding. He then returns to the main topic, explaining the two conditions in detail. First, the worshipper must know that the specific prayer he is performing (e.g., Fajr) is obligatory, even if he does not know which parts of that prayer are fard and which are sunnah. Second, he may believe that all rak'ahs he prays are obligatory (e.g., thinking both rak'ahs of Fajr are fard), and that suffices because supererogatory prayer can be fulfilled with the intention of obligatory (but not vice versa). The passage then quotes al-Tajnis wa al-Mazid: a person who prays the five prayers without knowing they are obligatory must repeat them; if he knows some are fard and some sunnah but cannot distinguish, his prayer is invalid unless he follows an imam; but if he mistakenly thinks all are fard, it is valid because the supererogatory is fulfilled by the intention of fard. Similarly, if he knows which are fard and which are sunnah overall but not within a single prayer, it is valid. The passage ends with an example of an imam leading a congregation while not knowing fard from sunnah: if he intends the whole prayer as fard, his own prayer is valid. For the congregation, prayers that have no sunnah before them ( 'Asr, Maghrib, 'Isha) are valid, but prayers with a preceding sunnah (Fajr, Zuhr) are not, because the imam's intention of fard for the whole prayer would include the sunnah rak'ahs, which is impermissible.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): Obligatory act; the passage distinguishes fard from sunnah and discusses the necessity of intending it.
سنة (sunnah): Recommended act; the opposite of fard in the context of prayer elements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person prays Asr but does not know whether Asr is fard or sunnah, and he intends it as sunnah, what is the ruling according to the passage?
A) Valid because intention of supererogatory suffices for obligatory.
B) Invalid because supererogatory intention cannot fulfill obligatory.
C) Valid because he prayed the correct number of rak'ahs.
D) Invalid because he missed a rukn.

Key Takeaway
For a fard prayer to be valid, the worshipper must either recognize the prayer as fard or believe all its rak'ahs are fard; supererogatory intention cannot substitute for obligatory.

The Final Sitting: Pillar or Condition?
Digression on the classification of the final sitting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on the final sitting and identify the practical consequence of whether it is a rukn or a shart.

Core Idea
The final sitting is a condition, not a pillar—so even if performed while asleep, the prayer is valid.

In this passage, the commentator delves into a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: is the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) a rukn (pillar) or a shart (condition)? The disagreement affects whether the prayer remains valid if one performs the sitting while asleep.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Some scholars say the final sitting is a shart (condition) while others say it is a rukn (pillar). The passage presents evidence from Munyat al-Musalli and Jami' al-Fatawa.
2
Examine the evidence
Sheikh Abdul Aziz al-Bukhari in al-Tahqiq explains that the final sitting is based on rest (istirahah), so sleep suits it, indicating it is a condition not a pillar.
3
Apply to the sleeping scenario
If someone sleeps through the entire sitting, according to Munyat al-Musalli he must sit when awake; if he does not, prayer invalid. But according to Jami' al-Fatawa, if he sits the duration while asleep, it is valid.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is: if the sitting is a shart, prayer is valid even if performed while asleep; if a rukn, it is not.
5
Understand the tarjih
The preferred view is that it is a shart, as indicated by the explanation in al-Tahqiq and the fact that it removes difficulty.

ومعرفة كيفية الصَّلاةِ، وما فيها منَ الخِصَالِ المفروضة على وجه يُمَيَّزُها عن الخصال المسنونة، أو اعتقاد أنها فرض، حتى لا يتنفّل بمفروض.
بخلاف القراءة لا يجدي نفعاً. وأما القعدة الأخيرة إذا فعلها نائماً ففي « منية المصلي» : إذا نام في القعدة كلها عليه أن يقعد إذا انتبه قدر التشهد، وإن لم يقعد فسدت صلاته، ويخالفه ما في «جامع الفتاوى : أنه لو قعد قدر التشهد نائماً يعتد به وعلله في التحقيق للشيخ عبد العزيز البخاري: بأنـهـا ليست بركن ومبناها على الاستراحة فيها فيلائمها النوم. انتهى. قلت: وهذا ثمرة الخلاف بين مــن قـال: إنها شرط ومن قال: إنها ركن فتصح نائماً على القول بشرطيتها لا ركنيتها، وهـو جـواب عـــن قـول صاحب البحر » اتفقوا على فرضية القعود الأخير واختلفوا في ركنيته، ولم أر من تعرض لثمرة هذا الاختلاف. انتهى فلله الحمد. (وَ) يشترط لصحة أداء الفرض أحد أمرين وهما أدنــي مـا يسقط بـه الفرض الأول منهما: (معرفة كيفية) أي: صفة ( الصَّلاةِ وَ) ذلك بمعرفة حقيقة (مَا فِيهَا ) أي : ما في جملة الصلوات ( مِنَ الخِصَالِ) أي: الصفات الفرضية يعني كونها فرضاً كاعتقاده افتراض ركعتي الفجر، وافتراض الأربع في الظهر، وأن سنة الصبح ركعتين غير الفرض، وأن سُنَّة الظهر أربعاً غير الفرض، وهكذا باقي الصلوات (المَفْرُوضَةِ) فيكون ذلك ( على وجه يُمَيِّزُها) بما ذكرنا وليس الشرط أن يميز ما اشتملت عليه صلاة الصبح ونحوها من الفرض والسنة، كأن يعتقد أن القيام ركن، والثناء والتعوذ سنة، والقراءة فرض والتسبيح سنة، بل المراد تمييز الصلاة في حدّ ذاتها بأنها فرض، فتتميز به ( عن الخِصَالِ) أي: الصفات (المَسْنُونَةِ) باعتقاد سُنّية ركعات يصليـهـا غـيـر الـفـرض، كالسـنـن الرواتب وغيرها. والثاني من الأمرين أشار إليه بقوله: (وَاعْتِقَادُ) المصلي (أنَّهَا) أي: إن الصلوات التي يؤديها كلها (فَرْضُ ) كاعتقاده أن جميع الأربع في الصبح فرض، والخمس في المغــرب فـرض، ويصلي ثلاث ركعات منها على حدتها. لأن النفل يصح بنية الفرض دون عكسه، وإليه أشار بقوله (حتى لا يتنفّلَ بمفروض باعتقاد أن ما فعله نفل قال في « التجنيس والمزيد » : رجل لم يعرف أن الصلوات الخمس فريضة على العباد إلا أنه كان يصليها -يعني: بلا نيتها في مواقيتها - لا يجوز وعليه أن يقضيها؛ لأنه لم ينو الفرض، والنية شرط. وكذلك إن علم أن منها ركعات فريضة ومنها سُنّة ولا يعرف الفريضة من السنة لم تجز لما قلنا، إلا إذا صلى خلف الإمام ونوى صلاته، وإن كان يظن أن كلها فريضة أجزأه ما صلى لأن النفل يتأدى بنيّة الفرض، أما الفرض لا يتأدى بنية النفل، وإن كان يعرف الفرائض من النوافل لكن لا يعلم ما في الصلاة أي ما في نفس الصلاة الواحدة من الفريضة والسنة، جازت صلاته؛ لأنه إذا عرف الفرائض ينوي الفرائض. انتهى.
ومثله في
:
والأركان من المذكورات أربعة: القيام، والقراءة، والركوع والسجود. وقيل: القعود الأخير مقدارَ التَّشَهْدِ. وباقيها شرائط، بعضُها شرط لصحةِ الشروع في الصَّلاةِ، وهو ما كان خارجها. وغيره شرط لدوام صحتها. الخلاصة» ثم قال: فلو أمّ قوماً وهو لا يعرف صلاة الفرض من صلاة النفل، ونوى الفرض الكُلّ جازت صلاته. وأما صلاة القوم فكل صلاة ليس لها سُنّة قبلها كالعصر والمغرب والعشاء تجوز، ولا تجوز صلاة القوم في كل صلاة لها سُنّة قبلها كالفجر والظهر.
And knowing the manner of prayer, and what it contains of obligatory qualities in a way that distinguishes them from recommended qualities, or believing that it is obligatory, so that one does not perform a supererogatory act with an obligatory act. Unlike reading, it is of no benefit. As for the final sitting, if one performs it while asleep, in 'Munyat al-Musalli': if he sleeps through the entire sitting, he must sit when he wakes up for the duration of the tashahhud, and if he does not sit, his prayer is invalidated. And it contradicts what is in 'Jami' al-Fatawa': that if he sits for the duration of the tashahhud while asleep, it is valid. And it is explained in 'al-Tahqiq' by Sheikh Abdul Aziz al-Bukhari: because it is not a pillar and its basis is rest, so sleep suits it. End. I say: This is the fruit of the dispute between those who say it is a condition and those who say it is a pillar: it is valid while asleep according to the view that it is a condition, not a pillar. And this is a response to the statement of the author of 'al-Bahr': 'They agreed on the obligatory nature of the final sitting and differed on its pillarhood, and I have not seen anyone who addressed the fruit of this difference.' End. So praise be to Allah. And for the validity of performing the obligatory prayer, one of two matters is required, the minimum by which the obligation is fulfilled. The first: knowing the manner of the prayer, and that is knowing the reality of what it contains of the obligatory qualities, meaning that it is obligatory, such as his belief that the two rak'ahs of Fajr are obligatory, and the four in Zuhr are obligatory, and that the sunnah of Fajr is two rak'ahs not obligatory, and the sunnah of Zuhr is four not obligatory, and so on for the rest of the prayers. The obligatory ones are such that he distinguishes them by what we mentioned. It is not a condition that he distinguish what the Fajr prayer and the like contain of obligatory and sunnah, such as that he believe that standing is a pillar, the thana' and ta'awwudh are sunnah, recitation is obligatory, and tasbih is sunnah. Rather, the intent is to distinguish the prayer in itself as being obligatory, so it is distinguished from the recommended qualities by believing that the rak'ahs he prays are sunnah aside from the obligatory, like the regular sunnah prayers and others. The second of the two matters is indicated by his statement: and the worshipper's belief that they are all obligatory, such as his belief that all four in Fajr are obligatory, and the five in Maghrib are obligatory, and he prays three rak'ahs of them separately. Because supererogatory prayer is valid with the intention of obligatory but not vice versa, and to this he pointed with his statement: so that he does not perform a supererogatory act with an obligatory act by believing that what he did is supererogatory. He said in 'al-Tajnis wa al-Mazid': A man who did not know that the five daily prayers are obligatory upon the servants, but he used to pray them (meaning without intention in their times) is not valid and he must make them up; because he did not intend the obligatory, and intention is a condition. Similarly, if he knows that some rak'ahs are obligatory and some are sunnah but does not distinguish the obligatory from the sunnah, it is not valid for what we said, unless he prays behind an imam and intends his prayer. And if he thinks that all are obligatory, his prayer suffices him, because the supererogatory is fulfilled by the intention of obligatory, whereas the obligatory is not fulfilled by the intention of supererogatory. And if he knows the obligatory from the supererogatory but does not know what is in the prayer (i.e., within the same prayer of obligatory and sunnah), his prayer is valid; because when he knows the obligatory, he intends the obligatory. End. And similar in: And the pillars from the mentioned are four: standing, recitation, bowing, and prostration. And it is said: the final sitting the extent of the tashahhud (is also a pillar). And the rest are conditions: some are conditions for the validity of beginning the prayer, which is what is outside it. And other is a condition for the continuity of its validity. Al-Khulasah. Then he said: If he leads a group while he does not know the obligatory prayer from the supererogatory prayer, and he intends the whole as obligatory, his prayer is valid. As for the prayer of the group, every prayer that has no sunnah before it, like 'Asr, Maghrib, and 'Isha, is permissible, and the prayer of the group is not permissible in every prayer that has a sunnah before it, like Fajr and Zuhr.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is a commentary on Nur al-Idah, dealing with the conditions for performing obligatory prayers validly. The commentator begins by stating the requirement that the worshipper must either know how to distinguish the obligatory elements (fard) from the recommended ones (sunnah) within his prayer, or believe that all the rak'ahs he is praying are obligatory. This is to prevent him from mistakenly performing a supererogatory prayer with the intention of obligatory, which would render it invalid. The commentator then digresses into a dispute about the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah). According to Munyat al-Musalli, if one sleeps through the entire sitting, he must sit up upon waking for the duration of the tashahhud; otherwise, the prayer is invalid. However, Jami' al-Fatawa holds that if he sits the duration while asleep, it is valid. The difference stems from whether the final sitting is a rukn (pillar) or a shart (condition). If it is a rukn, sleep invalidates it; if a shart, sleep does not harm because its basis is rest. The commentator (the author of Imdad al-Fattah) notes that this is the practical consequence (thamarah) of the ikhtilaf, which the author of al-Bahr had lamented not finding. He then returns to the main topic, explaining the two conditions in detail. First, the worshipper must know that the specific prayer he is performing (e.g., Fajr) is obligatory, even if he does not know which parts of that prayer are fard and which are sunnah. Second, he may believe that all rak'ahs he prays are obligatory (e.g., thinking both rak'ahs of Fajr are fard), and that suffices because supererogatory prayer can be fulfilled with the intention of obligatory (but not vice versa). The passage then quotes al-Tajnis wa al-Mazid: a person who prays the five prayers without knowing they are obligatory must repeat them; if he knows some are fard and some sunnah but cannot distinguish, his prayer is invalid unless he follows an imam; but if he mistakenly thinks all are fard, it is valid because the supererogatory is fulfilled by the intention of fard. Similarly, if he knows which are fard and which are sunnah overall but not within a single prayer, it is valid. The passage ends with an example of an imam leading a congregation while not knowing fard from sunnah: if he intends the whole prayer as fard, his own prayer is valid. For the congregation, prayers that have no sunnah before them ( 'Asr, Maghrib, 'Isha) are valid, but prayers with a preceding sunnah (Fajr, Zuhr) are not, because the imam's intention of fard for the whole prayer would include the sunnah rak'ahs, which is impermissible.

Key Terms
ركن (rukn): Pillar of prayer; omission invalidates the prayer intentionally.
شرط (shart): Condition for validity; outside the prayer (shurut al-in'aqad) or for continuity.
قعدة أخيرة (qa'dah akhirah): Final sitting in prayer, after which the prayer concludes with taslim.
تشهد (tashahhud): The testimony recited while sitting in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'fruit' (thamarah) of the dispute about the final sitting?
A) Whether the prayer is valid if one sleeps during the sitting.
B) Whether the sitting requires tashahhud.
C) Whether the sitting is sunnah or wajib.
D) Whether the imam's sitting is valid.

Key Takeaway
The final sitting's classification as a shart rather than a rukn allows it to be valid even if performed while asleep, due to its basis in rest.

Taharah — Purification

Prayer on Thick Felt with Impurity
Ruling on prayer on a thick felt with impurity on one side.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prayer on a thick felt (libd) with a pure top and impure bottom, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding its validity.

Core Idea
Thick felt is like two separate boards; impurity on one layer does not invalidate prayer on the other.

In this lesson, we examine a specific case of praying on a felt mat (libd) that has a pure upper surface but an impure lower surface. The Hanafi school, led by Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, rules that such prayer is valid because the felt's thickness effectively separates it into two independent layers. However, Abu Yusuf disagrees, considering it as a single object. We will explore the reasoning behind each position and identify the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The case is a felt mat (libd) whose upper side is pure and lower side is impure with a preventing impurity (najasah ghalizah).
The impurity is on the underside only.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, prayer on the pure upper part is valid (ja'iz). The prayer is not invalidated by the impurity below.
This is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Understand the legal analogy
The felt is likened to two separate boards (ka-annahu lawhan). Due to its thickness, the upper and lower layers are considered independent, so impurity on one does not affect the other.
The principle is that physical separation by thickness creates legal separation.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf disagrees: he considers the felt as a single object, so impurity anywhere on it renders the entire surface impure, making prayer invalid.
Abu Yusuf's view is based on treating the felt as one unit.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The text declares the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad as 'the correct view' (al-sahih), and this is the operative ruling for fatwa.
Tarjih is given by the commentator, aligning with the majority of the school.

النَّجِس بحركته، على الصحيح. ولو تنجس أحد طرفي عِمَامَتِهِ، فألقاه وأبْقَى الطَّاهر على رَأْسِهِ، وَلم يَتَحرَّكِ النَّجِسُ بَحرَكَتِهِ، جازَتْ صلاته، وإن تحرّك لا تجوز. وفاقد ما يُزيلُ به النجاسة ليس بحامل لها، ولا متلبس بها على الصحيح. ولو تنجس أحد طرفي عِمَامتِهِ أو ملحفته أو منديله (فَالْقَاهُ ) أي : الطرف النجس أي (وأبْقَى الطَّاهر على رأسه ولم يَتَحرَّك النَّجِسُ بَحرَكَتِهِ جازَتْ صلاته لعدم تلبسه بالنجس (وإنْ تَحَرَّكَ) الطرف النجس بحركته ( لا تَجُوزُ ) صلاته؛ لأنه حامل لها حكماً (۳) لاتصاله به إلا إذا لم يجد ساتراً غيره فيستتر بالطرف الطاهر منه، ولا يضره تحرك النجس بحركته للضرورة (وفاقد ما يُزيلُ به النجاسة) الكثيرة عن جسده وثوبه يصلي معها، ولا إعادة عليه؛ لأن التكليف بحسب الوسع. وفي النهاية » قال الإمام البقالي : فإن كان على بدن المصلي نجاسة لا يمكن غسلها إلا بإظهار عورتـه يصلي مع النجاسة؛ لأن إظهار العورة منهي عنه، والغسل مأمور به، والأمر والنهي إذا اجتمعـا كـان النهي أولى. قال في «البدرية» كلمة ما مقصورة غير ممدودة ليتناول المائعات سوى الماء أيضاً على قولهما، ولو قلت بالمد يصير إشارة إلى قول محمد : لأنه لا يجوز التطهير إلا بالماء لا بالمائعات كما في «مجمع الروايات»، (ولا) إعادة (على فاقد ما يستر عورته، ولو كان (حريراً) فإن وجد الحرير لزمه الصلاة فيه؛ لأن فرض الستر أقوى من منع لبس الحرير في هذه الحالة
Chapter on matters related to conditions of prayer and their branches. Prayer is permissible on felt, its upper surface pure and the lower impure. And on a pure garment with an impure lining, when it is not quilted, on a pure edge, even if the impure edge moves with his movement, according to the correct view. And if one end of his turban becomes impure, and he throws it off and keeps the pure part on his head, and the impure part does not move with his movement, his prayer is permissible; if it moves, it is not permissible. And one who lacks what removes impurity prays with it upon him, and there is no repetition upon him, nor upon one who lacks what covers his 'awrah, even if it is silk. … Chapter on matters related to conditions of prayer and their branches (1) • (Prayer is permissible) i.e., valid (on felt) (with kasra of lam and sukun of ba) (its upper surface pure, and its lower surface impure) with a preventing impurity; because due to its thickness it is like two garments, and it is like a thick board that can be separated into two boards, and its lower is impure—it is permissible on the pure according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf; because it is like two things on top of each other. (And) prayer is valid on a pure garment with an impure lining, when it is not quilted, because it is two separate garments, the pure placed over the impure. (And) it is valid on a pure edge of a mat, reed mat, or garment, even if the impure edge moves with his movement, because he is not a carrier of it, nor is he in contact with it according to the correct view. And if one end of his turban, or his wrap, or his handkerchief becomes impure, (and he throws it off), i.e., the impure end, (and keeps the pure on his head, and the impure does not move with his movement, his prayer is valid) because he is not in contact with the impurity. (And if it moves) the impure end with his movement, (his prayer is not valid) because he is a carrier of it in effect, due to its connection to him, unless he has no other covering, in which case he covers with its pure end, and the movement of the impure does not harm him due to necessity. (And one who lacks what removes impurity) the major impurity from his body and garment prays with it (upon him) and there is no repetition on him; because obligation is according to capacity. And in al-Nihayah: Imam al-Baqqali said: If there is impurity on the body of the praying person that cannot be washed except by exposing his 'awrah, he prays with that impurity; because exposing the 'awrah is forbidden, and washing is commanded, and when command and prohibition meet, the prohibition takes precedence. He said in al-Badriyyah: The word 'ma' (ما) is with a short alif (qasirah) not long (mamdudah) to include liquids other than water also according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And if you say it with a long alif, it becomes a reference to the opinion of Muhammad: because purification is only permissible with water, not with other liquids, as in Majma' al-Riwayat. (And) there is no repetition (on one who lacks what covers his 'awrah) even if (silk) is used. If he finds silk, he must pray in it because the obligation of covering is stronger than the prohibition of wearing silk in this situation.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This chapter (fasl) provides detailed rulings on praying with impure surfaces or garments. It begins by clarifying that prayer on a thick felt (libd) with only the bottom side impure is valid because its thickness makes it like two separate garments, a view held by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), contrary to Abu Yusuf who considers it as one object. Similarly, prayer on a pure garment with an impure lining is valid if the garment is not stitched or quilted, as it is effectively two separate layers. If a pure edge of a mat or garment is used, even if the impure part moves with the worshipper's movement, the prayer is still valid because the worshipper is not physically carrying the impurity (according to the correct view). For a turban or wrap: if one end is impure, the worshipper can remove that part and keep the pure part on his head; if the impure part does not move with his movement, prayer is valid; if it moves, it is invalid unless he has no other covering, in which case necessity allows it. Then the text addresses the person who has no means to remove impurity (e.g., no water): such a person prays with the impurity and does not need to repeat the prayer, because obligation is according to capacity. Imam al-Baqqali in al-Nihayah adds that if removing impurity would require exposing the 'awrah, the impurity is tolerated because prohibition takes precedence over command. The word 'ma' in the text is read short to include liquids other than water (according to the Sahibayn), while a long alif would restrict it to Muhammad's view that only water is valid. Finally, one who lacks a covering for the 'awrah should use anything, even silk, because covering is obligatory and silk prohibition is overridden by necessity.

Key Terms
لِبْد (libd): A thick felt or mat made of wool/hair, often used as a prayer mat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is prayer on a thick felt (libd) with an impure bottom considered valid by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) Because the impurity is on the bottom and not on the top.
B) Because the thickness makes it like two separate boards, so impurity on one layer does not affect the other.
C) Because the impurity is not visible from above.
D) Because the felt itself is pure by nature.

Key Takeaway
Thick felt is legally treated as two layers; prayer on the pure part is valid even if the other layer is impure.

Prayer on a Pure Edge When Impurity Moves
Ruling on praying on a pure edge when the impure part moves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on praying on a pure edge of a garment or mat when the impure part moves with the worshipper, and identify the conditions for validity.

Core Idea
If the impure part moves but is not carried by the worshipper, prayer is valid.

This lesson addresses a common scenario: a person prays on a mat or garment that has a pure area, but an adjacent impure part moves as the worshipper moves. The Hanafi school rules that as long as the worshipper is not physically carrying the impurity (i.e., it is not attached to him), the prayer remains valid. We will explore the distinction between being a 'carrier' of impurity and merely being in proximity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states that prayer on a pure edge of a mat, garment, or similar is valid even if the impure part moves with the worshipper's movement.
The passage uses 'ala tarafin tahir' (on a pure edge).
2
Understand the condition
The validity is conditional on the worshipper not being a 'carrier' of the impurity (laysa bihamil laha). The impurity must not be physically attached to his body or clothing in a way that he carries it.
The term 'hamil' (carrier) is key.
3
Analyze the movement
If the impure part moves because it is connected to the pure part that the worshipper is using, but the worshipper is not directly holding or carrying the impurity, the prayer remains valid. The movement alone does not transfer the impurity.
This is the 'correct view' (ala al-sahih) according to the text.
4
Contrast with attached impurity
The coming lesson on the turban will show the opposite: if the impurity is attached to the worshipper (e.g., on his turban) and moves with him, it invalidates prayer because he is then considered a carrier.
The distinction is between a separate object and an attached garment.

Chapter on matters related to conditions of prayer and their branches. Prayer is permissible on felt, its upper surface pure and the lower impure. And on a pure garment with an impure lining, when it is not quilted, on a pure edge, even if the impure edge moves with his movement, according to the correct view. And if one end of his turban becomes impure, and he throws it off and keeps the pure part on his head, and the impure part does not move with his movement, his prayer is permissible; if it moves, it is not permissible. And one who lacks what removes impurity prays with it upon him, and there is no repetition upon him, nor upon one who lacks what covers his 'awrah, even if it is silk. … Chapter on matters related to conditions of prayer and their branches (1) • (Prayer is permissible) i.e., valid (on felt) (with kasra of lam and sukun of ba) (its upper surface pure, and its lower surface impure) with a preventing impurity; because due to its thickness it is like two garments, and it is like a thick board that can be separated into two boards, and its lower is impure—it is permissible on the pure according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf; because it is like two things on top of each other. (And) prayer is valid on a pure garment with an impure lining, when it is not quilted, because it is two separate garments, the pure placed over the impure. (And) it is valid on a pure edge of a mat, reed mat, or garment, even if the impure edge moves with his movement, because he is not a carrier of it, nor is he in contact with it according to the correct view. And if one end of his turban, or his wrap, or his handkerchief becomes impure, (and he throws it off), i.e., the impure end, (and keeps the pure on his head, and the impure does not move with his movement, his prayer is valid) because he is not in contact with the impurity. (And if it moves) the impure end with his movement, (his prayer is not valid) because he is a carrier of it in effect, due to its connection to him, unless he has no other covering, in which case he covers with its pure end, and the movement of the impure does not harm him due to necessity. (And one who lacks what removes impurity) the major impurity from his body and garment prays with it (upon him) and there is no repetition on him; because obligation is according to capacity. And in al-Nihayah: Imam al-Baqqali said: If there is impurity on the body of the praying person that cannot be washed except by exposing his 'awrah, he prays with that impurity; because exposing the 'awrah is forbidden, and washing is commanded, and when command and prohibition meet, the prohibition takes precedence. He said in al-Badriyyah: The word 'ma' (ما) is with a short alif (qasirah) not long (mamdudah) to include liquids other than water also according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And if you say it with a long alif, it becomes a reference to the opinion of Muhammad: because purification is only permissible with water, not with other liquids, as in Majma' al-Riwayat. (And) there is no repetition (on one who lacks what covers his 'awrah) even if (silk) is used. If he finds silk, he must pray in it because the obligation of covering is stronger than the prohibition of wearing silk in this situation.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This chapter (fasl) provides detailed rulings on praying with impure surfaces or garments. It begins by clarifying that prayer on a thick felt (libd) with only the bottom side impure is valid because its thickness makes it like two separate garments, a view held by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), contrary to Abu Yusuf who considers it as one object. Similarly, prayer on a pure garment with an impure lining is valid if the garment is not stitched or quilted, as it is effectively two separate layers. If a pure edge of a mat or garment is used, even if the impure part moves with the worshipper's movement, the prayer is still valid because the worshipper is not physically carrying the impurity (according to the correct view). For a turban or wrap: if one end is impure, the worshipper can remove that part and keep the pure part on his head; if the impure part does not move with his movement, prayer is valid; if it moves, it is invalid unless he has no other covering, in which case necessity allows it. Then the text addresses the person who has no means to remove impurity (e.g., no water): such a person prays with the impurity and does not need to repeat the prayer, because obligation is according to capacity. Imam al-Baqqali in al-Nihayah adds that if removing impurity would require exposing the 'awrah, the impurity is tolerated because prohibition takes precedence over command. The word 'ma' in the text is read short to include liquids other than water (according to the Sahibayn), while a long alif would restrict it to Muhammad's view that only water is valid. Finally, one who lacks a covering for the 'awrah should use anything, even silk, because covering is obligatory and silk prohibition is overridden by necessity.

Key Terms
مُضَرّب (mudarrab): Quilted or stitched together, making two layers effectively one fabric.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is prayer valid when a pure edge is used and the impure edge moves?
A) Because the impurity is less than a dirham.
B) Because the worshipper is not carrying the impurity.
C) Because the impurity is on the bottom.
D) Because the movement is unintentional.

Key Takeaway
Prayer on a pure edge is valid if the worshipper is not carrying the impurity, even if the impurity moves.

Taharah — Purification

Prayer with an Impure Turban or Wrap
Ruling on prayer with a turban having an impure end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prayer when one end of a turban, wrap, or handkerchief is impure, and identify the condition regarding movement of the impure part.

Core Idea
If the impure end of your turban moves with you, you are carrying it and prayer is invalid.

This lesson focuses on a situation where a worshipper's head covering (turban, wrap, or handkerchief) has an impure end. The Hanafi school provides a detailed rule: if the worshipper removes the impure part and keeps the pure part on his head, and the impure part does not move with his movements, the prayer is valid. However, if the impure part moves (even slightly), the prayer becomes invalid because he is considered to be carrying the impurity. This ruling reinforces the concept of 'haml' (carrying) introduced in the previous lesson.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A person has a turban (or wrap, handkerchief) with one end impure. He removes the impure end and keeps the pure end on his head.
The impure part is no longer on his head, but it is still attached to the pure part.
2
State the ruling when the impure part does not move
If the impure part remains stationary (does not move with the worshipper's movements), the prayer is valid because he is not in contact with the impurity.
The condition 'lam yataharrak al-najis bi-harakath' (does not move with his movement).
3
State the ruling when the impure part moves
If the impure part moves with the worshipper's movement (e.g., during ruku or sujud), the prayer is invalid because he is considered a carrier of the impurity.
The impurity is attached to his body via the turban.
4
Identify the exception
If he has no other covering, he may use the pure part even if the impure part moves, due to necessity (darurah).
This is based on the principle of necessity overriding prohibition.

Commentary
This chapter (fasl) provides detailed rulings on praying with impure surfaces or garments. Key Terms
لِبْد (libd): A thick felt or mat made of wool/hair, often used as a prayer mat.
مُضَرّب (mudarrab): Quilted or stitched together, making two layers effectively one fabric.
فاقد ما يُزيل به النجاسة (faqid ma yuzilu bihi al-najasah): One who lacks any means (e.g., water, cloth) to remove impurity.

Taharah — Purification

Prayer When Unable to Remove Impurity or Cover 'Awrah
Concessions for prayer with impurity or lack of covering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi concession for one who lacks means to remove impurity or cover the 'awrah, and explain the reasoning based on capacity and necessity.

Core Idea
If you cannot remove impurity or find covering, pray as you are and do not repeat.

This lesson covers the final concessions in the text: if a person has no means to remove impurity from his body or clothing, or no means to cover his 'awrah, he prays in that state and does not need to repeat the prayer later. The Hanafi school bases this on the principle that 'obligation is according to capacity' (al-taklif bi-hasab al-wus'). Additionally, if removing impurity would require exposing the 'awrah, the prohibition of exposing 'awrah takes precedence over the command to purify.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The text deals with two separate but related cases: (1) one who lacks means to remove impurity from body or clothing, and (2) one who lacks means to cover his 'awrah.
Both cases involve incapacity (faqd).
2
State the ruling for impurity
The person prays with the impurity and does not repeat the prayer. Obligation is according to capacity.
The impurity must be on the body or garment, not on the prayer space.
3
State the ruling for covering 'awrah
The person prays with whatever cover is available, even if it is silk. The obligation of covering overrides the prohibition of silk.
Silk is normally prohibited for men, but it is permissible out of necessity.
4
Understand the nuance on the word 'ma'
The word 'ma' in 'ma yuzilu bihi al-najasah' (what removes impurity) is read with a short alif to include liquids other than water (according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). If read with a long alif, it would restrict to water (Muhammad's view in other sources).
This is a linguistic detail affecting the scope of permissible purifiers.
5
Apply the 'awrah case
If exposing the 'awrah is needed to remove impurity, the impurity is tolerated because prohibition takes precedence over command.
This is from Imam al-Baqqali's statement in al-Nihayah.

Source Text

لعدم تلبسه بالنجس (وإنْ تَحَرَّكَ) الطرف النجس بحركته ( لا تَجُوزُ ) صلاته؛ لأنه حامل لها حكماً (۳) لاتصاله به إلا إذا لم يجد ساتراً غيره فيستتر بالطرف الطاهر منه، ولا يضره تحرك النجس بحركته للضرورة (وفاقد ما يُزيلُ به النجاسة) الكثيرة عن جسده وثوبه يصلي معها، ولا إعادة عليه؛ لأن التكليف بحسب الوسع. وفي النهاية » قال الإمام البقالي : فإن كان على بدن المصلي نجاسة لا يمكن غسلها إلا بإظهار عورتـه يصلي مع النجاسة؛ لأن إظهار العورة منهي عنه، والغسل مأمور به، والأمر والنهي إذا اجتمعـا كـان النهي أولى. قال في «البدرية» كلمة ما مقصورة غير ممدودة ليتناول المائعات سوى الماء أيضاً على قولهما، ولو قلت بالمد يصير إشارة إلى قول محمد : لأنه لا يجوز التطهير إلا بالماء لا بالمائعات كما في «مجمع الروايات»، (ولا) إعادة (على فاقد ما يستر عورته، ولو كان (حريراً) فإن وجد الحرير لزمه الصلاة فيه؛ لأن فرض الستر أقوى من منع لبس الحرير في هذه الحالة
Chapter on matters related to conditions of prayer and their branches. Prayer is permissible on felt, its upper surface pure and the lower impure. And on a pure garment with an impure lining, when it is not quilted, on a pure edge, even if the impure edge moves with his movement, according to the correct view. And if one end of his turban becomes impure, and he throws it off and keeps the pure part on his head, and the impure part does not move with his movement, his prayer is permissible; if it moves, it is not permissible. And one who lacks what removes impurity prays with it upon him, and there is no repetition upon him, nor upon one who lacks what covers his 'awrah, even if it is silk. … Chapter on matters related to conditions of prayer and their branches (1) • (Prayer is permissible) i.e., valid (on felt) (with kasra of lam and sukun of ba) (its upper surface pure, and its lower surface impure) with a preventing impurity; because due to its thickness it is like two garments, and it is like a thick board that can be separated into two boards, and its lower is impure—it is permissible on the pure according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf; because it is like two things on top of each other. (And) prayer is valid on a pure garment with an impure lining, when it is not quilted, because it is two separate garments, the pure placed over the impure. (And) it is valid on a pure edge of a mat, reed mat, or garment, even if the impure edge moves with his movement, because he is not a carrier of it, nor is he in contact with it according to the correct view. And if one end of his turban, or his wrap, or his handkerchief becomes impure, (and he throws it off), i.e., the impure end, (and keeps the pure on his head, and the impure does not move with his movement, his prayer is valid) because he is not in contact with the impurity. (And if it moves) the impure end with his movement, (his prayer is not valid) because he is a carrier of it in effect, due to its connection to him, unless he has no other covering, in which case he covers with its pure end, and the movement of the impure does not harm him due to necessity. (And one who lacks what removes impurity) the major impurity from his body and garment prays with it (upon him) and there is no repetition on him; because obligation is according to capacity. And in al-Nihayah: Imam al-Baqqali said: If there is impurity on the body of the praying person that cannot be washed except by exposing his 'awrah, he prays with that impurity; because exposing the 'awrah is forbidden, and washing is commanded, and when command and prohibition meet, the prohibition takes precedence. He said in al-Badriyyah: The word 'ma' (ما) is with a short alif (qasirah) not long (mamdudah) to include liquids other than water also according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And if you say it with a long alif, it becomes a reference to the opinion of Muhammad: because purification is only permissible with water, not with other liquids, as in Majma' al-Riwayat. (And) there is no repetition (on one who lacks what covers his 'awrah) even if (silk) is used. If he finds silk, he must pray in it because the obligation of covering is stronger than the prohibition of wearing silk in this situation.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This chapter (fasl) provides detailed rulings on praying with impure surfaces or garments. It begins by clarifying that prayer on a thick felt (libd) with only the bottom side impure is valid because its thickness makes it like two separate garments, a view held by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), contrary to Abu Yusuf who considers it as one object. Similarly, prayer on a pure garment with an impure lining is valid if the garment is not stitched or quilted, as it is effectively two separate layers. If a pure edge of a mat or garment is used, even if the impure part moves with the worshipper's movement, the prayer is still valid because the worshipper is not physically carrying the impurity (according to the correct view). For a turban or wrap: if one end is impure, the worshipper can remove that part and keep the pure part on his head; if the impure part does not move with his movement, prayer is valid; if it moves, it is invalid unless he has no other covering, in which case necessity allows it. Then the text addresses the person who has no means to remove impurity (e.g., no water): such a person prays with the impurity and does not need to repeat the prayer, because obligation is according to capacity. Imam al-Baqqali in al-Nihayah adds that if removing impurity would require exposing the 'awrah, the impurity is tolerated because prohibition takes precedence over command. The word 'ma' in the text is read short to include liquids other than water (according to the Sahibayn), while a long alif would restrict it to Muhammad's view that only water is valid. Finally, one who lacks a covering for the 'awrah should use anything, even silk, because covering is obligatory and silk prohibition is overridden by necessity.

Key Terms
فاقد ما يُزيل به النجاسة (faqid ma yuzilu bihi al-najasah): One who lacks any means (e.g., water, cloth) to remove impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the prayer is not valid and must be repeated.
B) Because the obligation is according to capacity.
C) Because impurity is forgiven in all cases.
D) Because he prayed in a state of impurity intentionally.

Key Takeaway
If you cannot remove impurity or cover your 'awrah, pray as you are; the prayer is valid and no repetition is required.

Prayer on Felt and Garment with Mixed Purity
Ruling on mixed purity on prayer surface

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which prayer is valid on a felt or garment with mixed purity, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
Prayer is valid if the part in contact is pure, even if another part is impure, provided the two are considered separate.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah addresses a practical scenario: you have a prayer surface (like a felt rug or a garment) where part is pure and part is impure. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling based on the physical separation of layers. Understanding this helps avoid unnecessary invalidation of prayer when the impurity is not in contact with the worshipper.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objects and their state
Take note of the two examples: a felt (libd) and a garment (thawb). In each, one part is pure and the other impure (top vs bottom, outer vs lining).
2
State the default ruling
The matn says prayer is valid (tajuz) on the felt with pure top and impure bottom, and on the garment with pure outer and impure lining if not stitched together.
The ruling is based on the part in contact with the worshipper.
3
Identify the condition for validity
The condition is that the impure part is effectively separate from the pure part—either by thickness (libd) or by not being sewn together (ghayr mudarrab). This makes them like two layers.
If the garment is mudarrab (stitched/sewn), it is considered one unit and the ruling changes.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf disagrees: he considers the felt as one object, so impurity on any part invalidates prayer. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the validity.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: prayer is valid.
This is the view taken in the zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في متعلقات شروط الصلاة وفروعها
تجوز الصلاة على لبد وجهه الأعلى طاهر، والأسفل نجس. وعلى ثوب طاهر، وبطانته نجسة، إذا كان غير مضرب على طرف طاهر، خلافاً لأبي يوسف؛ لأنه كشيئين فوق بعضهما
Chapter on matters related to conditions of prayer and their branches. Prayer is permissible on a felt whose upper face is pure and lower face impure. And on a pure garment whose lining is impure, if it is not stitched together, [prayer is valid] on a pure edge, contrary to Abu Yusuf because it is like two things on top of each other.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents rulings on praying when the prayer surface has mixed purity. The matn (source text) states that prayer is valid on a felt (libd) if the top side is pure even if the bottom is impure, because the felt is thick and can be considered two separate layers. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold this view, while Abu Yusuf disagrees, considering it as one object. Similarly, if a garment has an impure lining but the outer is pure and the two are not sewn together (ghayr mudarrab), prayer is valid on the pure part. The commentary expands these rulings.

Key Terms
لبد (libd): A thick fabric made of felt or matted wool, considered thick enough to be like two layers.
مضرب (mudarrab): A garment that is stitched or quilted, making it one inseparable unit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is prayer valid on a felt (libd) whose top is pure but bottom is impure?
A) Because the impurity is not visible.
B) Because the felt is thick and considered separate layers.
C) Because the impurity is dry.
D) Because the bottom is not touched during prayer.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, prayer is valid on a surface with mixed purity if the part in contact is pure and the impure part is effectively separate (due to thickness or non-attachment).

Movement of Impurity During Prayer: Valid or Invalid?
Ruling on impurity movement during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prayer when an impure part of a garment moves, and apply the distinction between carrying impurity (hamil) and mere movement.

Core Idea
Prayer is valid if impurity moves but you are not carrying it; invalid if you are carrying it.

Imagine you are praying on a mat that has a small impure patch. If the mat shifts as you move, is your prayer still valid? The Hanafi school has a nuanced answer that depends on whether you are considered to be 'carrying' the impurity. This lesson unpacks that ruling, clarifies the key concepts of hamil and mutalabbis, and shows how necessity can override the general rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The text discusses praying on a pure edge of a mat, rug, or garment that also has an impure part. Note the two conditions: prayer is valid if the impurity moves but you are not carrying it (hamil) nor afflicted by it (mutalabbis).
2
Understand the Key Distinction
Contrast two cases: (1) The impure part moves because you move the entire object (e.g., the mat shifts) — you are not actively carrying the impurity, so prayer is valid. (2) The impure part is attached to your body (e.g., a turban end) and moves with your movement — you are considered to be carrying it, so prayer is invalid.
The term 'hamil' (carrier) is key: if the impurity is physically connected to you and moves as you move, you are deemed to be carrying it.
3
Apply the Exception for Necessity
The text mentions an exception: if you have no other cover (e.g., no other garment to wear), then you may use the pure part even if the impurity moves with you. This is due to necessity (darurah).
Necessity overrides the general prohibition.
4
Analyze the Example of the Turban
Consider a turban with one impure end. If you wrap only the pure part around your head and the impure end does not move when you move, prayer is valid. If it does move, prayer is invalid unless you have no alternative covering.
This illustrates the principle in a concrete, everyday scenario.
5
Review the Legal Maxim
The underlying maxim: 'What is attached to the body and moves with it is considered as carried.' This is a Hanafi principle that extends to similar cases (e.g., impure clothing).
Memorize this maxim for application in other chapters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتصح على طرف طاهر من بساط أو حصير أو ثوب وإن تحرك الطرف النجس بحركته لأنه ليس بحامل لها ولا متلبس بها على الصحيح. ولو تنجس أحد طرفي عمامته أو ملحفته أو منديله فألقاه وأبقى الطاهر على رأسه ولم يتحرك النجس بحركته جازت صلاته لعدم تلبسه بالنجس وإن تحرك الطرف النجس بحركته لا تجوز صلاته؛ لأنه حامل لها حكماً لاتصاله به إلا إذا لم يجد ساتراً غيره فيستتر بالطرف الطاهر منه ولا يضره تحرك النجس بحركته للضرورة
And prayer is valid on a pure edge of a mat, matting, or garment even if the impure edge moves with his movement, because he is not carrying it nor is he afflicted with it, according to the correct view. If one end of his turban, wrap, or handkerchief becomes impure, so he throws it away and keeps the pure part on his head, and the impure part does not move with his movement, his prayer is valid due to the absence of affliction with impurity. If the impure part moves with his movement, his prayer is not valid because he is considered to be carrying it due to its connection to him, except if he has no other cover, then he covers with the pure part, and the movement of the impurity does not harm him due to necessity.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling when a worshipper has an item of clothing or mat that has both pure and impure parts, and the impure part moves during prayer. The correct view is that if the impure part moves because the worshipper moves (e.g., the mat shifts), the prayer is still valid as long as the worshipper is not actively carrying the impurity. For example, if one end of a turban is impure and the worshipper throws that part away (wraps only the pure part), if the impure part does not move when he moves his head, prayer is valid. If it does move, it is invalid because he is considered to be carrying the impurity. However, if he has no other cover available, necessity allows using the pure part even if the impurity moves.

Key Terms
حامل للنجاسة (hamil li al-najasah): Being considered as actively carrying an impure object, which invalidates prayer.
متلبس (mutalabbis): Being physically in contact or afflicted with impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a worshipper has a turban with one impure end, and he wraps only the pure part, but the impure end moves slightly when he moves his head, what is the ruling?
A) Prayer is valid because he is not carrying the impurity.
B) Prayer is invalid because the impurity moves with his movement.
C) Prayer is valid only if the impurity does not move at all.
D) Prayer is invalid regardless of movement.

Key Takeaway
Prayer is invalid only when the impure part is carried (hamil) by the worshipper; mere movement without carrying does not invalidate.

Prayer When Unable to Remove Impurity
Ruling for one who lacks means to purify

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a person unable to remove impurity and explain the basis in the principle of obligation according to capacity.

Core Idea
Prayer with impurity is valid when removal is impossible, and no repeat is required.

This lesson covers a critical concession: when a person cannot remove impurity from his body or clothing—either because he lacks any purifier or because washing would expose his awrah—the Hanafi school rules that he prays with the impurity and does not repeat the prayer later. The basis is the foundational principle that obligations are only binding within one's capacity (al-taklif bi hasab al-wus'). The passage also touches on an intra-madhhab difference regarding which liquids qualify for purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two cases: (1) lacking any means to remove impurity, and (2) impurity that cannot be removed without exposing the awrah. Both lead to the same ruling.
2
Understand the primary ruling
The ruling is clear: pray with the impurity present, and no qada (repeat) is required. The basis is the principle 'al-taklif bi hasab al-wus''—obligation according to capacity.
This principle is derived from Qur'an 2:286.
3
Examine the awrah conflict
Imam al-Baqqali explains that when washing impurity would require exposing the awrah (which is prohibited), the prohibition takes precedence over the command to purify. So one prays without removing the impurity.
This is an application of the usul rule: when command and prohibition conflict, prohibition prevails.
4
Analyze the linguistic point
The commentator notes that the word 'ma' in the text has a short vowel (ma) meaning 'what' or 'that which', thereby including all liquids that can purify (e.g., vinegar, rosewater). If it were long (ma'), it would refer only to water, narrowing the scope to Muhammad's other view.
The short vowel reading supports the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفاقد ما يزيل به النجاسة الكثيرة عن جسده وثوبه يصلي معها ولا إعادة عليه؛ لأن التكليف بحسب الوسع. وفي النهاية قال الإمام البقالي: فإن كان على بدن المصلي نجاسة لا يمكن غسلها إلا بإظهار عورته يصلي مع النجاسة؛ لأن إظهار العورة منهي عنه والغسل مأمور به والأمر والنهي إذا اجتمعا كان النهي أولى. قال في البدرية: كلمة "ما" مقصورة غير ممدودة ليتناول المائعات سوى الماء أيضاً على قولهما، ولو قلت بالمد يصير إشارة إلى قول محمد: لأنه لا يجوز التطهير إلا بالماء لا بالمائعات كما في مجمع الروايات
And one who lacks something to remove the major impurity from his body and garment prays with it and there is no repetition upon him because obligation is according to capacity. In al-Nihaya, Imam al-Baqqali said: If there is impurity on the body of the worshipper that cannot be washed except by exposing his awrah, he prays with the impurity because exposing the awrah is prohibited while washing is commanded, and when command and prohibition meet, the prohibition takes precedence. He said in al-Badriyya: The word 'ma' is with a short vowel (ma) not long (ma') to include liquids other than water also according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). If you said with a long vowel (ma'), it would refer to the statement of Muhammad: because purification is only permissible with water, not with other liquids, as in Majma' al-Riwayat.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where a person cannot remove impurity from his body or clothes—either because he has no water or other purifier, or because removing it would require exposing his awrah. The ruling is that he prays with the impurity, and no qada (repeat) is required, because Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity. Imam al-Baqqali elaborates that if washing impurity would require exposing the awrah, the prohibition of exposing awrah takes precedence over the command to wash. The commentator also notes a linguistic point: the word 'ma' (ما) can either mean 'what' (short) or 'water' (long). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, 'ma' includes all liquids that can purify (like vinegar or rosewater), while Muhammad's other view restricts it to water. The text prefers the broader interpretation.

Key Terms
فاقد ما يزيل به النجاسة (faqid ma yuzil bihi al-najasah): One who lacks any means (water or other liquid) to remove impurity.
التكليف بحسب الوسع (al-taklif bi hasab al-wus'): Obligation is according to capacity; the principle that Allah does not oblige beyond one's ability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person finds that washing an impurity from his garment would require exposing his awrah, what should he do according to the Hanafi school?
A) He must expose his awrah to remove impurity and then pray.
B) He should pray without removing the impurity and not repeat later.
C) He should delay prayer until he can remove impurity privately.
D) He should perform tayammum for the impurity.

Key Takeaway
When a person cannot remove impurity due to lack of means or risk of exposing awrah, the Hanafi school permits prayer with impurity without requiring repetition.

The 'Awrah of a Free Woman: Face, Hands, and Feet Exempted
Ruling on the 'awrah of a free woman with proofs and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the 'awrah of a free woman (face, hands, feet exempted) and evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the back of the hand and the feet.

Core Idea
The free woman's 'awrah is all except face, hands, and feet – necessity (ibtila') exempts them, not inherent non-'awrah.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explains the ruling on a free woman's 'awrah, grounding it in Quranic and prophetic proofs. It then navigates important internal differences within the Hanafi school about the back of the hand and the feet. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correctly applying the rulings in prayer and daily life.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule and exceptions
Identify the rule: entire body of a free woman is 'awrah. List exceptions: face, hands, feet.
The term 'awrah here is defined as what must be covered in front of non-mahram men.
2
Analyze the primary proof from the Quran
Examine Surah al-Nur 24:31. Identify the phrase 'except what appears thereof' and how Hanafi scholars interpret it to exempt face and hands.
The proof from the Prophet's prohibition of gloves and niqab for muhrimah is used as supporting evidence.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the feet
Note the two narrations. Determine the sounder view (not 'awrah) based on necessity and the reasoning of al-Hidayah and al-Burhan.
The ikhtilaf also extends to outside prayer vs. prayer; the mufta bihi is that feet are not 'awrah in any context.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the back of the hand
Distinguish between Zahir al-Riwayah (back is 'awrah) and the chosen view (not 'awrah up to wrist). Understand the tarjih mechanism.
The hadith of Abu Dawud and the principle of necessity drive the chosen view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and conclusion
Consolidate: the operative position is that the entire hand (including back) and the feet are not 'awrah. Recognize the authority of later scholars in settling this ikhtilaf.
This understanding is crucial for issuing fatwas and for personal practice.

Source Text

وجميعُ بَدَنِ الحَرَةِ عَوْرَةٌ إلا وجهها وكفيها وقدَمَيْها. لأن لهما مزية ) والنظر إليها سبب الفتنة وما عدا ذلك منها فليس بعورة، لأن عمره كــان يضرب الإماء أن يتقنعن ويقول: ألق عنك الخمار يا دفار تتشبهين بالحرائر » (۳)، ولأنها تخرج لحاجة مولاها في ثياب مهنتها عادةً، فاعتبر حالها بذوات المحارم في حق الأجانب رفعاً للحرج، وقوله: يا دفـار بالدال المهملة وكسر الراء وتخفيف الفاء أي: يا منتنة، كذا في ( الحاوي». وروي أيضاً: « أَنَّ جَوَارِي عمره كُنَّ يَخدُمْنَ الصّيفانَ كاشفاتِ الرُّؤوس مضطربات النديين» (۳) كذا في « المستصفى» ومهنتها بكسر الميم وفتحها، وأنكر الأصمعي الكسر. (وجميعُ بَدَنِ الحُرَّةِ عَوْرَةٌ) في بعض النسخ: بــن الحــرة كلها وهـو تأكيد للبدن، والبدن مذكر إلا أنه أُضيف إلى المرأة أُعطي حكم التأنيث لا متزاج بينهما، وعليـه القــراءة الشاذة تَلْتَقِطْهُ بَعْضُ ولأن الاشتهاء لا يحصل بالنظر إلى القدم كما يحصل بالنظر إلى الوجه، فإذا لم يكن الوجه عورة مع كثرة الاشتهاء فالقدم أولى فإن قيل : قوله : ( المرأة عورة مستورة» (۲) عام في جميع بدنها وليس فيها استثناء، ليس داخـلا فـيـه انـتـهـى قــال شـارح المنية » : إنه مغلظ لأن إضافة الشيء إليه لا يقتضي عدم دخوله فيه، وإلا اقتضت إضافة الرأس إلى ...
And the entire body of a free woman is 'awrah except her face, her hands, and her feet. Because for these there is a special quality and looking at them is a cause of temptation. What is other than that from her is not 'awrah, because 'Umar used to strike the slave-girls to make them cover their heads and he would say: 'Remove your headcovering, O Dafar, you are imitating free women.' And because she goes out for the need of her master in the clothes of her usual work, so her condition is considered like that of mahram women in front of strangers to remove hardship. His saying: 'O Dafar' with the unpointed dal, kasr of the ra, and tashdid-less fa, meaning 'O stinking one' – as in al-Hawi. And it is also narrated: 'The slave-girls of 'Umar used to serve the guests with uncovered heads and swaying breasts' – as in al-Mustasfa. 'And her work' with kasr of the mim and fatha, and al-Asma'i denied the kasr. (And the entire body of a free woman is 'awrah) in some copies: 'her entire body' as an emphasis on the body, and the body is masculine but when added to woman it is given the ruling of femininity – no mixing between them, and upon that is the irregular reading 'taltaqitu ba'd al-sayyarah'. His saying: 'the breast of the spear was dyed with blood', and another's: [from the complete meter] 'When news of the vizier came, the walls of the city and the humble mountains lowered themselves' – as in al-Mustasfa. And His saying: 'delighting the beholders' [as in al-Mi'raj]. (Except her face, hands, and feet) due to His saying: 'And they should not display their adornment except what appears thereof' [Surah al-Nur 31], and what is meant is the place of their adornment, and what appears thereof is the face and the hands – said by Ibn 'Abbas and Ibn 'Umar, due to the tribulation of displaying them; and because he (peace be upon him) prohibited the muhrimah from wearing gloves and the niqab. If the face and hands were from the 'awrah, he would not have forbidden covering them with stitched garments. And regarding the foot, there are two narrations; the sounder of the two narrations is that it is not 'awrah, due to the tribulation of displaying it when she walks barefoot or in sandals, as she may not find a khuff – as in al-Hidayah and al-Burhan. Therefore he excepted it. And al-Aqta' said in his commentary on al-Sahih: It is 'awrah due to the apparent meaning of the hadith, which is his saying: 'The woman is a covered 'awrah' – end. Similar is in Majma' al-Riwayat. And in al-Ikhtiyar, regarding the foot, there are two narrations; the sound one is that it is not 'awrah in prayer and is 'awrah outside prayer – end. So the verification differs. And the verified conclusion is that the foot is not 'awrah due to what we mentioned, and because desire is not aroused by looking at the foot as it is aroused by looking at the face; if the face is not 'awrah despite much desire, then the foot is more worthy. If it is said: His saying: 'The woman is a covered 'awrah' is general in all her body and there is no exception in it, so excepting these limbs due to tribulation is a specification without its wording initially. It is permissibly answered: His saying: 'And they should not display their adornment' [Surah al-Nur 31] either came before or after the hadith. If it came after, then it abrogated the generality of the hadith. If it came before, then the hadith, being a solitary report, does not nullify anything that the verse covered. And Abu Dawud narrated a mursal hadith from him: 'When a girl reaches puberty, it is not permissible for anything to be seen of her except her face and her hand to the wrist.' And the verse does not contradict our statement that the foot is not 'awrah, because the place of the anklet is not the foot but the shank, as it is above the ankles, and the discussion is about the foot, and the need for displaying it is greater. And our statement: 'and her hands' includes both their outer and inner sides, as in the Mukhtalafat of Qadi Ghani and Qadi Khan: the outer side of the hand and its inner side are not 'awrah up to the wrist, and that is the chosen view. And in Zahir al-Riwayah, the outer side of the hand is 'awrah while its inner side is not 'awrah – end. And the explicit meaning of what Abu Dawud narrated is evidence that all of it is not 'awrah, and it is his saying: 'and her hand to the wrist.' And His saying: 'except what appears thereof' means: except what custom and nature has brought about its appearance, like the place of kohl – which is the eye, and the place of the ring – which is the finger, and what is meant by the eye is the face, and by the finger is the hand, which is applying the name of a part to the whole as in al-Mustasfa. And this repels what he said: that his statement in al-Hidayah: 'and her hand' indicates that the back of the hand is 'awrah. And the statement of al-Kamal: that adding 'the outer' to the name of the hand requires that it – i.e., the back of the hand – is not included in it – end. The commentator of al-Minya said: This is incorrect, because adding something to something does not require its non-inclusion; otherwise, adding the head to ...
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil) from the Sharh of Nur al-Idah. It states the general rule: the entire body of a free woman is 'awrah (must be covered before non-mahram men), except the face, hands (including both palms and backs, with some ikhtilaf), and feet. The author presents evidence from the Qur'an (Surah al-Nur 31: 'except what appears thereof') and from hadith (the Prophet's prohibition of gloves and niqab for a muhrimah indicates these are not 'awrah, because covering them would then be required). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the feet: one narration says they are 'awrah, but the sounder view (as expressed in al-Hidayah and al-Burhan) is that they are not, due to necessity (when walking barefoot). Similarly, regarding the back of the hand (zahir al-kaf), the preferred view (al-mukhtar) is that it is not 'awrah up to the wrist, but in Zahir al-Riwayah (the core transmitted books) the back is considered 'awrah while the palm is not. The author prefers the view that includes both back and palm as non-'awrah, citing the hadith of Abu Dawud and the flexibility of custom.

Key Terms
عورة ('awrah): The body parts that must be covered before non-mahram men; for a free woman, it is all except face, hands, and feet.
كف (kaf): The hand, including both palm and back, up to the wrist; there is ikhtilaf about the back of the hand.
قدم (qadam): The foot; there are two narrations within Hanafi: not 'awrah or 'awrah; the sounder view is that it is not 'awrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi Hanafi position in this passage, which part of a free woman's body is considered 'awrah outside of prayer?
A) The face and hands
B) The entire body except the face, hands, and feet
C) The entire body including the face and hands
D) Only the private parts

Key Takeaway
The free woman's 'awrah is all except face, hands, and feet; the mufta bihi includes both palm and back of hand up to wrist and the feet as exempted, based on necessity and prophetic practice.

The 'Awrah of a Woman: Forearms and Flowing Hair in Hanafi Fiqh
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on a woman's 'awrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi position on the 'awrah of a woman's forearms and flowing hair, and identify the key intra-Hanafi disputes and their evidence.

Core Idea
A woman's forearms and flowing hair are 'awrah in the relied-upon Hanafi view, but significant intra-madhhab ikhtilaf exists.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah delves into the precise definition of a woman's 'awrah, focusing on two contested areas: the forearms (dhira') and the flowing part of the hair (mustarsil). While the Zahir al-Riwayah holds both are 'awrah, alternative narrations and practical considerations create a nuanced discussion. This lesson will guide you through the evidence and the tarjih process that yields the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested body parts
The passage explicitly debates the status of the forearm (dhira') and the flowing hair (mustarsil) as 'awrah. Recognize these as the focal points.
2
State the Zahir al-Riwayah position
According to the core Hanafi texts (the six books), both forearms and flowing hair are 'awrah. This is the default transmission from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
Zahir al-Riwayah carries more weight in the Hanafi school.
3
Note the opposing narrations
Abu Yusuf narrated from Abu Hanifa that forearms are not 'awrah. Abdullah al-Balkhi held that flowing hair is not 'awrah. Additionally, some scholars (e.g., in al-Ikhtiyar) treat the forearm as not 'awrah inside prayer due to need.
These are from non-Zahir al-Riwayah sources.
4
Examine the evidence
The hadith 'al-mar'atu 'awrah' is used to include hair, as the Prophet only explicitly exempted the face and hands. The passage also argues that flowing hair is either a limb or an adornment; in either case, it is not from the apparent adornment (like jewelry) that is exempted.
The hadith has zanni thubut and dalala, allowing for ikhtilaf.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Based on tarjih by Abu al-Layth and al-Hidayah, the relied-upon position is that both forearms and flowing hair are 'awrah. However, note the practical concession in al-Ikhtiyar for forearms if uncovered due to necessity.
The fatwa is upon the view that flowing hair is 'awrah.

زيد عدم دخول الرأس في مسمى زيد وكما يقال: ظاهر الكف كذلك يقال: باطن الكف فدفعه مدفوع، وسلوك « الكافي» » مسلك الضرورة في التعليل ظاهر في أن ظاهر الكف ليـس بـعــورة كباطنه؛ لأن الضرورة على إبداء ظاهره أشد من باطنه فكان أصح وإن كان غير ( ظاهر الرواية»، وأشرنا إلى أن ذراع الحرة عورة، وهو «ظاهرة الرواية عن أئمتنا الثلاثة، وفي غير ظاهر الرواية» عن أبي يوسف أنه روى عن أبي حنيفة أن ذراعيها ليسا بعورة وفي ( المبسوط» في الذراع روايتان، والأصح أنــه عــورة كذا في «الفتح» وفي ( الاختيار » : لو انكشف ذراعُها جازت صلاتها؛ لأنها من الزينة الظاهرة وهـو الـســوار وتحتاج إلى كشفه في الخدمة كالطبخ والخبز وستره أفضل انتهى. قال الكمال: وصحح بعضهم أنه عورة في الصلاة لا خارجها. واعلم أنه لا تلازم بين كونه ليس بعورة وجواز النظر إليه فحل النظر منـــوط بـعـدم خشية الشهوة مع انتفاء العورة، ولذا حرم النظر إلى وجهها ووجه الأمرد إذا شك في الشهوة مع ا العورة؛ ولذا حرم النظر إلى وجهه ولا عورة. انتهى. وشمل ما وراء المستثنى أيضاً شـعـر الـحــة وفـي كــون المسترسل من شعرها عورة روايتان وفي ( المحيط الأصح أنه عورة، وفي « الهدايــة هـو الصحيح وبـه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث وعليه الفتوى، كما في جامع المحبوبي ) وهو احتراز عن رواية « المنتقى » ليس بعورة وبه قال «عبد الله البلخي » ، كما في ( الدراية» والتحقيق أن يقال: لا يخلو إما أن يفرض الشعر النازل عضواً أو زينة خلقية، فإن كان الأول فكونه عورة ظاهر؛ لأن النبي الله إنما استثنى الوجه والكفين خاصة فيكون الشعر داخلاً في قوله : ( المرأة عورة ) وسقوط غسله عنـهـا، عـلـى مـا مـر؛ للحـرج لا لأنه ليس منها، وإن كان الثاني فكذلك لأنه ليس من الزينة الظاهرة ليكون من المستثناة، بل من الخفية كالسوار، والخلخال، والدملج، والقلادة"، والإكليل، والوشاح، والقرط، وذكــر الـزينـــة دون مواقعها للمبالغة في الأمر بالصون والستر؛ ولأن هذه الزينة واقعة على مواضع معلومة من الجسد، لا يحل النظر إليها لغير من استثنى الله فهي عن إبداء الزينة نفسها ليكون أرسخ في حرمة إبداء مواقعها.
